ix 
 
Abstract 
The increased focus on sustainability have led to demands for biogenic products in our buildings and timber 
is now more widely considered as load-bearing structures replacing noncombustible structures of steel and 
concrete. Furthermore, our buildings tend to grow taller and wider challenging the limits for the validated 
field of fire safety engineering which rely on well-documented models based on small compartments with 
noncombustible structures. Introducing a combustible material as timber in a large compartment will alter 
the fire dynamics significantly. Despite the field is under ongoing investigation it still demands thorough 
research. Today, this field put high demands on the fire safety consultant dealing with buildings where 
exposed timber is present. 
The scope of this thesis is to investigate how travelling fires in a large fire compartment with partially 
exposed timber behave under different ventilation conditions.   
Initially the thesis gives a short historical approach of timber as a building material followed by a 
description of the behaviour of wood subject to fire. The Danish fire regulations for buildings is briefly 
summarized.  
The main content of the thesis is based on CFD-simulations using Fire Dynamics Simulator (FDS) ver. 6.7.9. 
A total of 16 different scenarios were carried out. The FDS-model was a large compartment with height 5 m 
and floor area 15x45 m² with opening factors 0.05 m½ and 0.10 m½. This size of compartment exceeds the 
area of validity of the models for standard fire compartments.  
The exposed timber was either located at the ceiling or the walls. The amount of exposed timber was 10 % 
and 20 % with respect to the total surface area of the walls and ceiling of the compartment. The HRR of the 
timber was 100 kW/m² and 150 kW/m². 
In all scenarios the timber influenced the behaviour of the travelling fire. Timber located at the ceiling led 
to a flashover occurrence at an early stage, while location of the timber on the walls farthest from the 
initial fire did not affect the travelling fire until either the travelling fire approached and ignited the wood, 
or the hot smoke layer led to ignition of the wood. 
The opening factor had a high impact of the overall fire dynamics in the compartment leading to lower 
temperatures. On the other hand, in some scenarios the high opening factor led to more combustion of the 
fuel leading to a higher HRR.  
All scenarios demonstrated inhomogeneous conditions in the large compartment with the peak 
temperatures following the position of the travelling fire. The scenarios also demonstrated than in a large 
compartment there are regions each with their own individual conditions. It is not recommended to use 
one model to cover the whole area in a large compartment. 
The thesis revealed that further investigations are necessary, and these fields of research are highly 
recommended in future studies.  These research topics are listed at the end of the thesis. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:11==
 
 
 
x 
 
Resumé (dansk) 
Det øgede fokus på bæredygtighed har medført en øget efterspørgsel på biogene byggematerialer i vores 
bygninger, og træ bliver nu mere og mere almindeligt at benytte som bærende konstruktioner som 
erstatning for ikke brændbare konstruktioner af stål og beton. Desuden bliver vores bygninger stadig højere 
og bredere hvilket udfordrer hele det fundament, som den kendte brandtekniske viden hviler på, hvor der 
er tale om små brandrum og ikke brændbare byggematerialer. Når man indbygger et brændbart materiale 
som træ i et stort rum, vil branddynamikken blive væsentligt ændret. Selvom der foregår studier af hele 
området, er der stadig behov for yderligere viden og forskning. I dag stilles der store krav til den 
brandtekniske rådgiver, der beskæftiger sig med bygninger med synlige trækonstruktioner.  
Formålet med denne afhandling er at undersøge, hvordan vandrende brande i store brandceller med 
delvist eksponerede trækonstruktioner udvikler sig under forskellige ventilationsforhold.  
Indledningsvis gives der en kort historisk introduktion til træ som byggemateriale efterfulgt af en 
beskrivelse af de brandtekniske egenskaber for træ. Brandkravene i det danske bygningsreglement er 
derefter kort beskrevet.  
Hovedparten af afhandlingen er baseret på CFD-simuleringer ved brug af Fire Dynamics Simulator (FDS)  
ver. 6.7.9. I alt 16 scenarier blev gennemført. FDS-modellen var et stort rum med højde 5 m og gulvareal 
15x45 m². Åbningsfaktorerne var 0.05 m½ og 0.10 m½. Brandrum i denne overskrider grænsen for, hvornår 
de normale modeller for brandrum kan anvendes.  
Det synlige træ var enten placeret på loft eller vægge. Andelen af træets overflade udgjorde 10 % og 20 % i 
forhold til brandrummets samlede væg- og loftoverflader. Træets brandeffekt var 100 kW/m² og 
150 kW/m².  
Alle scenarier viste, at den vandrende brand blev påvirket af træet under brandforløbet. Var træet placeret 
på loftet opstod der flashover på et tidligt stadie af branden. Træ placeret på væggene længst fra den 
initiale brand påvirkede først den vandrende brand senere i brandforløbet; enten når branden nærmede sig 
træet, som derefter blev antændt, eller når der var udviklet tilstrækkeligt med varme røggasser til at disse 
kunne initiere en antændelse af træet.   
Åbningsfaktoren havde stor indflydelse på branddynamikken og gav anledning til lavere temperaturer. 
Omvendt gav en høj åbningsfaktor i nogle scenarier mulighed for, at der kunne finde en større forbrænding 
sted, som udløste en højere brandeffekt.  
Alle scenarioer viste, at der i et stort brandrum er store temperaturforskelle, hvor den maksimale 
temperatur følger den vandrende brands position. Scenarierne viste også, at der i et stort brandrum er 
lokale områder, som har hver deres egne betingelser. Det kan derfor ikke anbefales at bruge én model til at 
dække et stort brandrum.  
Under udarbejdelsen af afhandlingen dukkede der emner op, som kræver yderligere undersøgelser. Det 
anbefales derfor, at disse emner afdækkes i fremtidige studier. De er nævnt til sidst i denne afhandling.

1 
Background 
1.1 
Motivation 
The construction development towards biogenic building materials moves fast, and the architects are 
constantly pushing the limits for the building industry to produce even wider, more open and taller, 
innovative buildings. 
The theoretical and empirical models used for fire engineering are developed on the basis on 
noncombustible structures and small compartments and are normally used as basis in a performance-based 
fire design. This collides with the designs of contemporary buildings with large open spaces and visible 
unprotected timber structures. Continuing to use these basic design models in a context where they do not 
fully apply, and in an environment with a significant altered fire scenario with burning timber, could 
severely jeopardize the fire safety of our buildings.  
Fortunately, this topic is being widely discussed and has awareness all over the world among fire safety 
engineers and considerable literature and results from full-scale experiments have already been published.  
This thesis is just a tiny piece in this big puzzle. My hope is that fire science engineering and sustainability 
can go hand in hand in the future uniting the best of both worlds.  
1.2 
Purpose and research questions 
The purpose of this thesis is to investigate how travelling fires behave in a large compartment with exposed 
timber, and how altering the ventilations conditions and the amount and location of the exposed timber 
affect the behaviour of the travelling fire.  
The thesis considers and investigates the following questions. 
• 
How does the presence of exposed timber influence the behaviour of a travelling fire in a large 
compartment? 
o How does the amount of the exposed timber influence the behaviour? 
o How does the location of the exposed timber influence the behaviour? 
o Under which conditions is an amount of exposed timber of 20 % a safe fire design? 
• 
How do the ventilation conditions influence the behaviour of a traveling fire in a large 
compartment? 
o For a given amount and location of exposed timber, how do the ventilation conditions 
influence the behaviour of the travelling fire? 
o How do the locations of the openings affect the fire spread? 
• 
Under which conditions is flashover a realistic scenario? 
• 
What are the conditions in a large compartment with exposed timber regarding temperatures and 
heat release rate? 
• 
Does the exposed timber alter the behaviour of the fire spread and the combustion of the 
moveable fire load?  
• 
Is the assumption of uniform conditions a safe fire design in a large compartment? 
 
 
==PAGE:14==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
2 
 
 
1.3 
Thesis limitation and authors remarks  
This thesis investigates the fire dynamics in a large compartment with travelling fires under the influence of 
exposed timber and different ventilation conditions. The personal safety of occupants and evacuation 
conditions are not a part of this thesis, nor are issues regarding the fire brigade considered. These aspects 
are however always considered in the fire safety strategy in a real building design.  
The increased amount of fuel in the compartment due to exposed timber causes a ventilation-controlled 
fire and external combustion at a higher level than in a noncombustible building. This external flaming and 
risk of vertical fire spread is not a part of this thesis but should be considered in any fire design with 
exposed timber.  
The results and conclusions stated in this thesis are only applicable for the actual scenarios investigated. 
Any change of the parameters e.g., location of the initial fire, charring rate, amount and location of exposed 
timber, compartment geometry and opening factor will evidently lead to other results. The results and 
conclusions stated in this thesis should therefore only serve as guidelines and cannot without further 
investigations be directly applied to other cases.  
The investigations are based on CFD-simulations. To set up a manageable model, boundary conditions and 
simplicities have been necessary to define and apply. These conditions are based on data from codes and 
literature but need not represent actual conditions in a real fire scenario.  
The reference list includes all the literature the author has investigated during the making of this thesis. 
References that are not directly quoted in the text have been used as overall background information for 
better understanding of travelling fires and exposed timber subject to fire.  
Where suitable, supplementary comments are added to the text as notes in Italic. 
The opinions expressed in this publication are solely those of the author.  
 
 
==PAGE:15==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
3 
 
 
2 
Introduction 
The increased focus on climate changes and sustainability combined with political goals of reduced  
CO2-emissions have led to demands of biogenic products as building materials. Timber is now more widely 
considered as a building material replacing masonry, steel and concrete, either as massive, 
unmanufactured structures or as glue laminated timber (GLT) and cross laminated timber (CLT).  
Traditionally, timber has primarily been used for simple buildings at limited height, but during the last 
decades multi-storey and high-rise buildings in timber have occurred more often [1].  
 
 
Figure 2.1 Examples of contemporary buildings with exposed timber constructions. Left: Sara Kulturhus in Skellefteå, 
Sweden. Multi-purpose building with a 20-storey hotel with load-bearing CLT- and GLT-elements. Design by White 
Architects [2]. Right: Interior of WoodHub, Government Office Hub, Odense Denmark. Visible columns in solid timber and 
partially visible CLT floor constructions are used as load-bearing and recurring construction materials. Design by C.F. 
Møllers Architects [3].  
Timber as a construction material has many advantages e.g., low self-weight, easy on-site assembly, 
suitability for pre-manufacturing, low thermal conductivity and aesthetical benefits. However, introducing 
exposed timber in our buildings may have to alter the way we handle fire engineering. As opposed to 
masonry, steel and concrete, timber is a combustible material. In the event of a fire the timber will, if not 
properly protected, contribute to the fire load in the building leading to a more severe and longer fire. In a 
fire safety context this is very important to consider if we wish to maintain the same level of fire safety in 
our buildings regardless of combustible or noncombustible construction materials and structures are being 
used.  
The existing theories for fire, smoke and temperature evolvement in buildings originates from minor 
standard (often cubic) fire compartments of noncombustible materials. These theories have been proven 
valid and are widely used all over the world for fire engineering purposes. Modern buildings often have 
wide open spaces or multi-storeys in open connection with each other. These geometrical premises 
challenge the well-known theories and lead to more unpredictable fire propagations and fire dynamics than 
in small standard compartments.   
In a long, narrow compartment a local fire can travel through the compartment creating non-uniform 
conditions in the compartment. Over the recent years, experiments have been carried out in large 
==PAGE:16==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
4 
 
 
compartments [4] [5] and analytical formulas have been derived [6] [7] to describe this phenomenon 
known as a ‘travelling fire’. The knowledge on the field is however still limited, especially when exposed 
timber is present. The uncertainties regarding exposed timber constructions in large open spaces ultimately 
challenges the theory that lies beneath our concept of fire engineering.  
The increasing demand for mass timber constructions requires a new way of thinking in building design to 
maintain an adequate level of fire safety of buildings in the future. It is essential that the designer of the 
building possesses very good skills in both material, fire and structural engineering science, which is a rare 
combination since many engineers and designers are highly experienced in a narrow field of engineering.  
If not considered properly and fully understood, the use of mass timber constructions in buildings may lead 
to a building with a level of fire security below acceptance regarding both safety of the occupants, 
constructions, and the fire brigade.  
 
 
==PAGE:17==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
5 
 
 
3 
A short historical approach 
To understand the purpose behind the restricted regulations regarding timber structures and how wood 
and timber have been used in our buildings, a short historical approach is suitable.  
3.1 
Fuel source 
Wood has since dawn of mankind been used as a fuel source for heating and food preparation purposes. In 
the early years of the last century before electricity and gas was common in households, wood was used as 
a fuel source in the wood burning stove for preparing food and in the fireplace for heating. Wood was also 
used to heat the water in the big boiler kettle when doing laundry. 
Nowadays, wood as a fuel source in most industrial countries has been replaced with either electricity, 
natural gas, district heating or oil, and the green agenda has introduced new technologies such as heat 
pumps. Wood stoves and fireplaces are still present, but in our contemporary well insulated houses they 
serve more as ambient sources to cosiness.  
3.2 
Construction material 
Wood as a construction material has for centuries been used in buildings, either as load carrying members 
or as interior and exterior cladding and lining. Wood has also been used for infrastructural purposes e.g., 
bridges and railroad ties. In the following the term ‘timber’ is used to describe manufactured wood used for 
building purposes. 
Before the industrialisation timber was the primarily used construction material for buildings. The buildings 
were simple and traditional with 1 or 2 storeys. The walls either consisted of massive timber or were 
constructed as half-timbering consisting of wooden frames with load-carrying members and infills of a 
noncombustible material, see Figure 3.1.  
   
 
Figure 3.1 Examples of half-timbering houses with exposed timber in façades. Photos by the author from “Den Gamle By”, Århus. 
When the population in the capitol Copenhagen increased because of the industrialisation and economic 
growth a new way of thinking housing were necessary, and multi-storey buildings began to rise. Buildings 
with half-timbering with exposed wooden structures were common until the beginning of the 19th century. 
However, the great fires of Copenhagen in 1728 and 1795 demonstrated in all its cruelness how vulnerable 
==PAGE:18==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
6 
 
 
buildings with exposed combustible structures were, and many buildings and irreplaceable cultural 
treasures were lost in the fires. Consequently, exterior facades with combustible structures were forbidden 
by a royal decree and only facades with noncombustible materials were allowed [8]. Half-timbering could 
still be used as interior walls. In 1889 with the implementation of the Building Law of Copenhagen half-
timbering were no longer a possibility [8] due to the fire risk. 
In October 1871 two large fires took place during the same days on the other side of the Atlantic Ocean: 
The Peshtigo Fire and the Great Chicago Fire. The Peshtigo Fire was a large forest fire that led to over a 
million affected acres and killed more than thousand people [9]. The Great Chicago Fire killed 
approximately 300 people and destroyed roughly 9 km2 of the city. The prevalent wooden structures in the 
city were one of many reasons that led to this disaster [10].  
The above examples from the past tell us that exposed timber in relation to fire should be considered very 
carefully.  
 
 
 
==PAGE:19==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
7 
 
 
4 
Characteristics of wood 
Before investigating, predicting and understanding the fire dynamics in a compartment where exposed 
timber is present, we need to understand the properties of wood.  
4.1 
Combustion and igniting of wood 
Wood is a biogenic product made from photosynthesis and is retrieved from forests by felling of trees. It 
primarily consists of cellulose, hemicelluloses and lignin. The chemical formula of the wood depends on the 
actual specie, but as an overall rule the composition with respect to mass is about 50 % carbon, 6 % 
hydrogen and 44 % oxygen (C6H9O4) [11]. 
4.2 
Pyrolysis 
When wood is subjected to sufficient external heat a rather complex chemical process named pyrolysis 
starts. Pyrolysis is defined as a process by which a solid (or a liquid) undergoes thermal degradation into 
smaller volatile molecules, without interacting with oxygen or any other oxidants [12].  
During pyrolysis, volatile gases formed by the decomposition of the wood are released from the surface, 
see Figure 4.1. These gases interact with each other and the oxygen in the air thus building flammable 
mixtures of gases. Given the temperature and surrounding oxygen fraction is at a sufficient level, the gases 
will ignite. The actual ignition temperature depends on multiple factors of the wood, such as specie, 
density, thermal properties, moisture content etc. In a fire design context, an autoignition temperature of 
300 °C is used as a parameter for ignition of wood [13]  [14]. 
During pyrolysis, energy (heat) is released giving even more energy to the combustion process as a sort of 
chain reaction. 
 
 
Figure 4.1 Left: Combustion phases of wood exposed to external heat [15]. Right: Different zones in a wood section 
subject to fire exposure [16] 
 
 
 
==PAGE:20==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
8

1) Some literature states 280 °C [17].  
 
For temperatures above 100 °C water starts to evaporate. This vaporization also entails an increase in the 
moisture content inside the wood, as the residual moisture is moving towards the core of the wood when 
the surface is exposed to fire. The increase of the moisture content in the wood can be as much as twice as 
the initial moisture content [20]. 
The formation of char at the surface starts at 300 °C. Below the char layer there is a heated layer of wood. 
The zone with temperatures between 200 °C and 300 °C is known as the pyrolysis zone [14], see Figure 4.1. 
The inner protected core of the wood is not subject to any heat exposure.  
The combustion of wood is either with visible flames (flaming combustion) or non-visible flames (glowing or 
smouldering combustion). In all stages the wood undergoes thermal degradation and loss of strength.  
Flaming combustion with visible flames has the highest burning and heat release rate and is the most 
important process with respect to the fire spread [17]. Glowing or smouldering combustion has a lower 
burning and heat release rate [21] , but is still a process where the wood decomposes with loss of cross 
section (charring) and strength.  
4.3 
Self-extinction (auto-extinction) 
There is no exact definition of the condition “self-extinction” (or auto-extinction) of wood [13] [21] why the 
word should be used with care. In some literature self-extinction is defined by the authors as flame 
extinction [22], but self-extinction may also describe the full extinction of any wood combustion where 
neither glowing nor smouldering combustion no longer takes place.  
==PAGE:21==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
9 
 
 
When dealing with self-extinction of wood it is essential to define and describe the scenario. Self-extinction 
defined by flame extinction may lead to the wrong assumption, that no reignition of the wood can take 
place. But under given circumstances the wood can reignite, for instance if the oxygen content or air flow 
increases [19] or if the charred protective layer falls off and fresh wood is exposed. If a self-extinguishment 
scenario is likely it must rely on no delamination or char layer fall-off [23]. 
Wood is not able to maintain burning without an external heat source applied to the surface [19, 21]. The 
heat flux needed to sustain burning or combustion of the wood depends on the conditions and time of 
exposure. Experiments carried out on wood samples indicate a maximum incident heat flux for extinction is 
30 kW/m² [22]. These tests were carried out on small wood samples and the results should not without 
further investigation be applied to full-scale compartments.  
The location of the exposed timber is also of importance when dealing with self-extinction. The thermal 
feedback between surfaces facing each other can lead to sustained flaming [21]. This could be the case in 
corners with adjacent timber surfaces affecting each other. 
Recent full-scale tests carried out with exposed CLT ceiling [4] showed that the flaming combustion of the 
ceiling ceased few minutes after the fuel (in this case consisting of wood cribs) was burnt out, but the 
glowing and smouldering combustion continued for several days at so called hot spots. This indicates once 
again than predicting and incorporating a design with self-extinction should be done with outmost 
cautiousness. 
4.4 
Burnout 
In a compartment with noncombustible structures and linings, the fire is expected to cease when all the 
fuel in the room has been consumed and the fire is prevented from spreading to other areas and 
compartments. This is described as “burnout” [13]. Designing for burnout is when the structures are 
designed to maintain their load-bearing capacity during the pre-assigned time, normally the time needed to 
evacuate the building and securing proper intervention from the fire brigade. The taller and more complex 
a building, the more time is needed. However, the term “burnout” needs to be explicitly defined in the 
given context as there is not exact definition of the scenario [13].  
When combustible, exposed structures are introduced in a compartment, the design for burnout gets 
complex. The structures itself will contribute to the fuel in the compartment altering the fire dynamics in 
the room. Even after all the moveable fuel has been consumed, the structures can maintain burning 
undergoing either flaming or glowing/smouldering combustion.  For a burnout design to be successful the 
structures must achieve self-extinction before the load-bearing capacity of the structures is exceeded which 
can be challenging to predict. It is not certain if a structure will completely combust in the event of a fire or 
if the structure undergoes self-extinguishment and the fire decays [23]. 
In fact, in a fire design context the burnout (meaning all combustion has completely ceased) can only be 
assumed to take place by manually intervention and fire extinguishing [13].  
4.5 
Charring  
As described in the previous section charring of wood can be expected to take place at temperatures above 
300 °C. Charring is a well-known phenomenon for all who have watched a bonfire or a fireplace, where the 
surface of the wood darkens and gets fissures and cracks. The char layer possesses very different thermal 
properties than the normal unaffected wood because of the decomposition and gas evaporation.  
==PAGE:22==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
10 
 
 
Due to the low density and low thermal inertia of the char layer, it acts as an insulation layer protecting the 
underlying wood from further combustion, or at least reduces the speed of the charring rate. This is 
however not considered in the design codes [14]. The char layer has no strength and cannot be considered 
when designing load-bearing timber structures.  
It is important to note that if the char layer falls off and fresh wood gets visible, a new combustion process 
can take place leading to a rise in the heat release rate in the compartment. When using CLT-members this 
is a very important issue since delamination due to the failure of the temperature-sensitive adhesive 
between the layers can occur. This has been documented in fire tests e.g., [24]. If not considering this in the 
design, the fire safety could easily be compromised.  
4.5.1 
Charring rate 
The speed at which the char layer develops from the surface and towards the inner core of the wood is 
referred to as the charring rate (β) measured in mm/min. The charring rate depends on many factors e.g., 
the type of wood, density, moisture content, grain orientation, permeability, thickness of the element and 
the level of thermal exposure (heat flux) just to mention some of them [16] [25]. The higher density and 
moisture content the lower charring rate [14], while the charring rate increases with the level of the heat 
flux [25].  
When designing structures for fire the charring rate is an important factor. The charring rate determines 
how much of the initial cross section that can be considered after a given period corresponding to the load-
bearing fire resistance requirement, normally 30, 60 or 120 minutes. 
The charring rate also determines the rate of combustion of the wood and thus the contribution to the fire 
load and HRR in the compartment. This is however rather complicated since these are co-dependent and 
iterative methods much be used in a fire design in order to determine the total fire load [26], see Figure 
4.2. 
 
 
Figure 4.2 Illustration of iterative calculation process for determining the fire 
load contribution due to charring [27] 
 
Figure 4.3 Reduced cross section method 
according to DS/EN 1995-1-2 for standard 
fire exposure [28]. 
For structural design purpose of mass timber constructions, a constant charring rate is normally used. 
When designing for standard fire exposure using the reduced cross section method, design values of 
β = 0.70 mm/min for laminated wood and β = 0.8 mm/min for conifer are used in the Danish national code 
[29].  
==PAGE:23==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
11 
 
 
The charring rates in the national codes are based on uniform temperature scenarios and a standard fire 
exposure. In a compartment with a travelling fire and non-uniform conditions the charring rate would be 
significantly changed and influenced by the actual fire scenario [30].  
Large-scale tests carried out [21] [31] have demonstrated a wide range of charring rates, also referred to as 
pyrolysis rates, for CLT-elements. Depending on the location of the timber (ceiling or lateral wall) values of 
the charring rates ranged from 0,4 mm/min to 2.3 mm/min. The charring rate is highly influenced by the 
oxygen level. Excess of pyrolysis gases prevents sufficient mixing with oxygen which slows down the 
pyrolysis and charring rate [21]. This non-uniform burning and charring of the exposed timber surfaces 
makes it very difficult to determine a representative charring rate of the timber in a fire design context.  
Intermediate-scale fire test under standard fire exposure demonstrated char rates of 0.83 ± 0.20 mm/min 
[32]. These values should only under critical assessment be applied to a fire design given the test conditions 
which do not apply in a compartment fire under very different conditions depending on the characteristics 
of the compartment. 
For non-uniform fires (travelling fires) under different exposure conditions and sizes of the travelling fire, 
the charring depth based on computer simulations in a 45.5 m long compartment have been found to reach 
a stable level with a maximum char depth of 40 mm [30]. These simulations were based on a fire load of 
540 MJ/m2. 
Full scale experiments carried out in a 352 m² (floor area) facility with a fully exposed CLT-ceiling revealed 
char depth of 15 – 40 mm depending on the location and fire exposure conditions [4].  
The above observations and experiments demonstrate that a default charring rate and a maximum char 
depth is highly unpredictable as is depends on multiple factors. This emphasizes once again than in a fire 
design context with exposed timber surfaces careful assessment regarding the fire load and heat release 
rate contribution from the timber should be made. Pre-defined values for charring rates are allowed as a 
part of the Danish national code (except for CLT), but in a fire design context the designer should always 
consider if the conditions for using these values are present. As the values are based on a standard fire 
exposure the use of the values in a performance-based design with other fire exposures should be 
questioned [30]. 
4.5.2 
Zero-strength layer 
The pyrolysis zone of the cross section is known as the zero-strength layer. Due to the elevated 
temperatures in this zone and the reduction of the strength of the timber, the zone is disregarded in the 
structural fire design. According to DS/EN 1995-1-2 [28] the thickness of the zero-strength layer is 7 mm, 
see Figure 4.3. 
Previous studies and experiments have shown rather larger deviations from these standard design values, 
especially regarding travelling fires, where values of 12 mm have been found [30], which under some 
circumstances could jeopardize the fire safety  [33]. The values are as previously mentioned based on a 
standard fire exposure, and a real fire will most likely differ a great deal from this assumption, especially if 
exposed timber structures are present.  
==PAGE:24==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
12 
 
 
For parametric fire exposures recent work questions the use of the charring rates according to DS/EN 1995-
1-2. Numerical analysis and results based on experiments showed that the current method given in DS/EN 
1995-1-2 is unconservative [34]. 
Another important aspect is that CLT-members nowadays are widely used in mass timber constructions due 
to their many benefits e.g., easy assembly and suitability for pre-fabrication off-site. The Danish national 
code does not include CLT-members and the fire design of the structures shall therefore be documented by 
other guidelines.  
Since the charring rate together with the zero-strength layer determines for a given fire duration e.g., 60 
minutes, the total reduction of the cross section, it is utmost important that correct or conservative values 
are used. Otherwise, the load-bearing capacity of the structure in the fire situation could be overestimated.   
Note: To this authors knowledge there is ongoing work in revising the building codes to incorporate CLT-
members and more realistic values of charring rates and zero-strength layer.  
4.6 
Thermal and mechanical properties 
Wood subject to heating will undergo changes that affect the thermal and mechanical properties. Beneath 
the charring layer, even if no pyrolysis occurs, the temperature as well as the moisture content has a great 
impact on the load-bearing capacity of the structure.  
During the last decades many experiments have been carried out all with the same result. A rather rapid 
decrease of strength and stiffness with increased temperature, even as low as 50 °C. [35] [36]. This is 
incorporated in the national codes for designing timber structures in the fire load situation, see Figure 4.4 
and Figure 4.5. 
 
Figure 4.4 Reduction factor for strength parallel to grain of 
softwood according to DS/EN 1995-1-2 [28]. 
 
Figure 4.5. Effect of temperature on modulus of elasticity 
parallel to grain of softwood according to DS/EN 1995-1-2  [28]. 
The thermal properties also vary significantly with increased temperature due to the combustion and 
chemical changes of the wood and the impact of the moisture content.  This has been widely analysed and 
depending on the model and assumptions different values are found, see Figure 4.6 and Figure 4.7. 
==PAGE:25==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
13 
 
 
 
Figure 4.6 Temperature-thermal conductivity relationship for 
wood and the char layer according to DS/EN 1995-1-2 [28]. 
 
Figure 4.7 Temperature-specific heat relationship for wood and 
charcoal [28]. At 100 °C water evaporates leading to the 
vertical increase of the value.  
It is further complicated by the fact that the density and the moisture content and the load in relation to 
the grain are parameters that influence the strength of the timber during fire and how quickly charring 
occurs [37] [38]. 
4.7 
Timber as a load-bearing structure 
Timber as a load-bearing structure subject to heat and fire will undergo physical changes. The charring 
diminishes the cross section thus reducing the load-bearing capacity of the element. Simultaneously, the 
heat impact from the fire exposure decreases the strength and stiffness of the structure, see Figure 4.4 and 
Figure 4.5. It is important to notice, that this strength and stiffness reduction takes places at temperatures 
far below the pyrolysis at 300 °C i.e., before the wood is combusting. This also means, that even though the 
timber is protected with a fire protective cladding, the temperature rise beneath the cladding reduces the 
strength and stiffness of the timber. The purpose of the protective cladding is to ensure, that the timber 
does not contribute to the fuel in the compartment in the prescribed time e.g., 60 minutes.  
If the fire protective cladding fails during the fire, the charring rate of the preheated wood will be higher 
than the charring rate of an initially exposed timber structure [28]. 
 
 
==PAGE:26==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
14 
 
 
5 
Danish Building Regulations 
This chapter briefly describes the Danish Building Regulations regarding fire safety to set the thesis into a 
national context.  
The evolution of the Danish fire regulations for buildings are based on experiences from fire incidents and 
political demands and agendas. Taking new technologies into account is also a factor when the regulations 
are revised. After a severe fire incident, the circumstances are always thoroughly investigated and if 
needed the building regulations are adjusted to maintain a sufficient and accepted level of fire safety. In 
modern times the severe fires ‘Hotel Hafnia’ in 1973 with 35 fatalities and ‘Stengade’ in 1975 with 9 
fatalities led to fire regulations with retroactively effect in Denmark.  
5.1 
Methods of documentation 
Since 2004 the Danish Building Regulations have been performance based. To fulfil and document the fire 
safety requirements, different approaches and methods such as approved guidelines, pre-accepted 
solutions or fire dimensioning based on calculations and simulations can be used.  
Today, the method of documentation of fire safety must be determined based on one of the following 
categories [39]: 
1) Pre-accepted solutions which provide examples of fire protection solutions which comply with the 
fire safety requirements of the building regulations. 
2) Fire-technically justified assessment. 
3) Comparative analysis based on pre-accepted solutions. 
4) Fire dimensioning. 
5) Fire test(s). 
6) A combination of the five methods stated above. 
Depending on the risk class of the building and the method of documentation a certified fire consultant 
must be associated with the project. 
5.2 
Timber structures as a pre-accepted solution 
Up until 2004 timber structures in multi storey buildings, except for single-family houses, were not allowed 
as a pre-accepted solution, and only noncombustible structures were pre-accepted. In 2004 the Danish 
Building Regulations introduced timber structures as an approved solution in multi storey buildings with the 
height to the top floor not exceeding 9.6 m above ground [40]. The 9.6 m was the limit for the fire brigade 
to use hand carried rescue ladders. The condition was that the building either was fully automatically 
sprinklered or that the timber structures were fully protected with a fire protection system to ensure, that 
the timber did not contribute to the fire load in the building in the first 60 minutes of a standard fire.  
In 2021 the pre-accepted solutions were adjusted, and according to the present pre-accepted solutions, 
timber as a load carrying member can be used in buildings where the height of the top floor is maximum 
12.0 m above ground [41]. This corresponds to a building with 4-5 storeys above ground, depending on the 
floor height.  
The structure must maintain its load-bearing capacity during fire for at least 60 minutes (R60). In order not 
to contribute to the fire, the timber structures must be properly protected either by cladding according to 
DS/EN 1995-1-2 [28] or by a fire protection system K2 60 /A2-s1,d0.  
==PAGE:27==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
15 
 
 
Unprotected surfaces of the load-bearing combustible structures are allowed if the total area of the 
exposed surfaces, within the first 60 minutes of the standard fire, does not exceed 20 % of the total surface 
area (walls and ceiling) of the compartment. The 20 % is the limit where the contribution from the exposed 
timber structures to the overall fire load in the building is assumed to stay at an acceptable level for a 
standard compartment. As an alternative to a fire protective cladding an automatically sprinkler system is 
an option.  
5.3 
Methods of documentation outside the pre-accepted solutions 
It is possible to design buildings higher than 4-5 storeys with load-bearing members of timber. Since the 
buildings are not included in the pre-accepted solutions other methods of documentation must be used, 
see section 5.1. A proper documentation will typically include fire and evacuation simulations based on 
probable fire scenarios and sensitivity analyses. It is vital in this documentation that both the fire consultant 
and the structural engineer fully understands the nature of the fire dynamics when exposed timber is 
present.  
 
 
==PAGE:28==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
16 
 
 
6 
Fire dynamics in standard compartments 
In this chapter the theory of fire dynamics for standard compartments is briefly described. This 
fundamental knowledge is the key to understand how (and why) the behaviour of a travelling fire in a large 
compartment differs and to process and investigate the output from the CFD-simulations properly. For a 
thorough explanation and understanding of the topic is referred to the acknowledged literature e.g., 
Karlsson/Quintere [42], Quintere [43] or Drysdale [44]. 
6.1 
Stages of an unsuppressed fire 
A fire evolving in a standard fire compartment where no fire suppression takes place will undergo five 
stages, see Figure 6.1: 
• ignition 
• growth 
• flashover 
• fully developed (steady state) 
• decay (cooling phase) 
 
 
Figure 6.1. Fire development in a standard compartment [45]. 
When designing for evacuation and personal safety the early stages (ignition and growth) are considered. 
This is often a time span of maximum 30 minutes depending on the tenability criteria. 
When designing for structures and stability of the building the fully developed fire during the steady state 
and decay phase are of interest. This is often when 30 minutes have been exceeded.  
To understand the fire dynamics of each stage, we need to know the basic fire dynamics and definitions 
regarding a fire compartment.  
6.2 
Opening factor 
The opening factor of a compartment is defined as [42] 
O = 
𝐴𝑜√𝐻𝑜
𝐴𝑡
 
Ao is the total area of the openings [m2] 
Ho is the weighted height of the openings [m] 
At is the total surface area of the compartment including openings [m2] 
==PAGE:29==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
17 
 
 
The opening factor determines the extent of air entrainment and release of hot gases and thus the level of 
oxygen and whether the fire is fuel-controlled or ventilation-controlled. An increase of the opening factor 
will lead to a shorter fire duration with higher temperature while a decrease of the opening factor will lead 
to a longer fire duration with lower temperatures. This was one of the findings of Magnussen and 
Thelandersson, and the calculated temperature–time curves have been widely replicated in acknowledged 
literature regarding fire design e.g., Enclosure Fire [42] and SFPE Handbook of Fire Protection Engineering 
[45]. 
In modern buildings the windows are not prone to break in case of a fire since modern glass windows 
consists of 3-4 layers of glass. This should be considered when designing the fire safety of a building as the 
available openings highly affects the fire propagation in the compartment.  
Previous work regarding fire design of compartments have been based on numeral assumptions, one of 
them being a ‘traditional’ fire compartment of a limited size and height where the fire evolves through the 
five stages, see section 6.1. In a large compartment the fire might behave differently.  
6.3 
Ventilation-controlled and fuel-controlled fires 
A fire can only take place in an environment where enough oxygen is present since oxygen is necessary to 
start any combustion process. If no oxygen is present, or the fraction of oxygen drops below the level 
needed for combustion, the combustion will either not take place or cease. This is one of the basic 
knowledge areas regarding fire and the fire triangle. 
A fire can either be fuel-controlled or ventilation-controlled. In a fuel-controlled fire the amount of oxygen 
in the compartment is sufficient to ensure the full potential of the fuel to combust. In a ventilation-
controlled fire the oxygen in the compartment limits the fire to a level where only as much fuel as the 
oxygen level allows can combust, where the oxygen level depends on the compartment geometry, the 
openings and air entrainment possibilities.   
The heat release rate for a fuel-controlled fire 𝐻𝑅𝑅𝐹𝐶 is defined as [45] 
𝐻𝑅𝑅𝐹𝐶= ∆𝐻𝑐,𝑒𝑓𝑓 ∙𝑀𝐿𝑅 
∆𝐻𝑐,𝑒𝑓𝑓 is the effective heat of combustion [kJ/kg] 
MLR is the Mass Loss Rate [kg/s] 
The HRRFC can be determined by calorimeter tests. 
 
The maximum energy release rate 𝑄̇ 𝑉𝐶 (𝐻𝑅𝑅𝑉𝐶) of ventilation-controlled fire can be expressed as 
(‘Kawagoe formula’) [42] 
𝑄̇ 𝑉𝐶= 𝐻𝑅𝑅𝑉𝐶=  0.09 ∗𝐴𝑜√𝐻𝑜∗∆𝐻𝑐,𝑒𝑓𝑓,𝑤𝑜𝑜𝑑 
∆𝐻𝑐,𝑒𝑓𝑓,𝑤𝑜𝑜𝑑 is the effective heat of combustion for wood [kJ/kg] 
 
 
==PAGE:30==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
18 
 
 
The heat release rate of a fire never exceeds the limit for the ventilation-controlled fire no matter how 
much fuel the compartment contains. 
Fuel-controlled fire:  
HRRFC  < HRRVC = 0.09 ∗𝐴𝑜√𝐻𝑜∗∆𝐻𝑐,𝑒𝑓𝑓,𝑤𝑜𝑜𝑑 
Ventilation-controlled fire:  HRRFC  > HRRVC = 0.09 ∗𝐴𝑜√𝐻𝑜∗∆𝐻𝑐,𝑒𝑓𝑓,𝑤𝑜𝑜𝑑 
Whether the fire is fuel-controlled or ventilation-controlled has a high impact on the fire progress. Large 
openings and a high opening factor allow for a fuel-controlled fire to take place, whereas a small opening 
factor results in a ventilation-controlled fire. A fuel-controlled fire is normally characterized by higher 
temperatures and shorter fire duration compared to the ventilation-controlled fire. It is always the most 
restrictive condition (lowest HRR) that determines whether a fire is fuel-controlled or ventilation-
controlled, see Figure 6.2. 
 
Figure 6.2. Illustration of a fuel-controlled and ventilation-
controlled fire with the same amount of fuel. In this case the 
fire is ventilation-controlled since HRRmax > HRR2 = HRRVC  
[46]. 
In the growth phase the amount of oxygen is under normal conditions sufficient for a fuel-controlled fire to 
take place. As the fire evolves and fully develops the oxygen is consumed. When the air entrainment is 
limited by the openings, the fire evolves from being fuel-controlled to ventilation-controlled. This is the 
case at flashover. 
6.4 
Growth phase 
For design purposes a so called ‘t-squared fire’ is often used to describe the growth phase after ignition 
[47], where the heat release rate 𝑄̇  [kW] is expressed as 
𝑄̇ =  𝛼𝑡2 
α is the fire growth coefficient [kW/s2] 
t is the time [s] 
The growth coefficient α depends on the type of fuel burning. The literature provides values for different 
types of fuels based on calorimeter tests see e.g., Table 40.1 in SFPE Handbook of Fire Protection 
Engineering [45].  
==PAGE:31==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
19 
 
 
A fire growth is either rated as slow, medium, fast or ultra-fast based on the time for the heat release rate 
𝑄̇  to reach 1,055 kW [47], see Table 6.1.

6.5 
Flashover and steady state 
When flashover takes places all combustible material in the compartment is assumed to be involved in the 
fire and flames can be seen coming out of the openings due to excess of fuel (ventilation-controlled fire). 
The exact prediction of flashover to take place will depend on the actual circumstances, but a flashover 
scenario is often assumed to happen when the gas temperature in the compartment reaches 500 °C – 
600 °C [48]. At this stage the heat release rate has reached its maximum level and the fire enters a steady 
state that will last as long as there is fuel available or until the fire is suppressed by either the fire brigade or 
an automatic sprinkler system.  
6.6 
Decay phase 
When all the combustible fuel has burnt out the fire will start to decay. The decay phase is assumed to be 
linear. The duration of the decay phase depends on the initial amount of the fuel and how much fuel that 
has been consumed during the growth phase and the steady state phase.  
6.7 
Total energy release 
During the fire the total energy release depends on the amount of the fuel in the compartment. 
Q = Q1 + Q2 + Q3 
Q = Total energy release [kJ] 
Q1 = Energy released during the growth phase [kJ] 
Q2 = Energy released during the steady state phase [kJ] 
Q3 = Energy released during the decay [kJ] 
Q1 = ∫

An example of a fire design curve for a fuel-controlled fire is presented in Figure 6.3.

6.8 
Post-flashover fires (fully developed fires) 
When designing structures for fire load the fully developed fire is considered. According to the Danish 
building regulations [39] the fire resistance of load-bearing building parts are determined either by the 
standard fire according to ISO 834 [49] or by use of a natural fire process (parametric fire) according to 
DS/EN 1991-1-2 with national annex [50] [51] and DS/INF 1991-1-2 [52]. 
The standard fire curve and the parametric fire curve were developed for small fire compartments and are 
not immediately applicable for large open spaces. Both models assume homogeneous gas temperatures in 
the compartment which can be a valid assumption in a small compartment. In a real fire, the temperature 
varies spatially, but a homogenous temperature condition allows us to simplify our models [42].  
6.8.1 
Standard fire 
The standard fire curve represents a design fire primarily applied to determine the fire resistance of various 
elements of construction as a tool for classification and comparison purposes but is also used as input in 
various calculation methods. It is well known that the standard fire curve does not reflect a real fire 
scenario, as it does not consider a cooling or decay phase and is independent of the properties of the fire 
compartment and the actual fire load. 
When designing structures exposed to fire, the standard fire is often used as building regulations and pre-
accepted solutions for load-bearing structures are based on this fire exposure by determining the fire 
resistance classes as e.g., R30, R60 or R120. 
The standard fire exposure curve is defined in ISO 834 [49] as  
𝑇= 345 ∙𝑙𝑜𝑔10(8𝑡+ 1) + 20

t is the time [min]

It is important to notice that the standard fire exposure not always gives a conservative approach, and the 
actual compartment geometry, fire load and properties should therefore always be considered.  
The furnace testing for classification of a combustible structure based on the standard fire has some 
important implications. This is briefly discussed in section 7.1. 
6.8.2 
Parametric fire 
The parametric fire is a more realistic design fire describing a fully developed fire with a cooling phase 
taking both the opening factor, the fire load and the thermal inertia of the fire compartment into account. 
This method can be used as a design for structures exposed to fire.  
According to the Danish national code [51], the time-temperature curve for the whole duration of the fire 
can be expressed as

7 
Fire load contribution from timber 
Introducing exposed timber in a compartment contributes to the fire load and impacts the heat release rate 
which highly affects the overall fire dynamics. The total amount of the fire load in a compartment consists 
of the fire load from the moveable load, the fire load from the permanent load and the fire load from the 
timber structures. 
The total fire load density pr. unit floor area 𝑞𝑓,𝑡𝑜𝑡 [MJ/m²] can be expressed as 
𝑞𝑓,𝑡𝑜𝑡= 𝑞𝑓,𝑚+ 𝑞𝑓,𝑝+ 𝑞𝑓,𝑡  
𝑞𝑓,𝑚 is the moveable fire load 
𝑞𝑓,𝑝 is the permanent fire load 
𝑞𝑓,𝑡 is the fire load from the timber structures or lining  
The fire load from the timber structures is also considered a part of the permanent fire load, but for 
transparency reasons it is shown separately here. 
Introducing more fuel in a compartment increases the heat release rate and affects the development of the 
fire. In Figure 7.1 an HHR-curve for a standard fire compartment with exposed timber (red lines) is 
illustrated compared to the case with no exposed timber (black lines). 
The HRR is elevated due to more combustible material, given enough oxygen (fuel-controlled fire) or in a 
ventilation-controlled scenario, the burning is extended. The figure also illustrates the importance of 
assessing the risk of char layer fall-off or delamination, at this will lead to a reinstatement of the flaming 
combustion and increase of HRR. 
 
Figure 7.1 Modified HRR-curve for a compartment with exposed timber (red 
lines) compared to a HHR-curve for a standard compartment with no exposed 
timber present (black lines) [53]. 
7.1 
Fire resistance test for fire classification 
As described in section 4.5.1 the charring rate of wood depends on the actual fire exposure conditions. A 
more severe fire with higher temperatures will lead to a higher values of charring rate and char depth than 
a fire with a lower temperature at the same given time [16].  
During a fire resistance test in a furnace for a combustible element e.g., a timber column or a timber beam, 
heat will be released from the element itself as it undergoes combustion, see section 4.1. This heat 
==PAGE:36==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
24 
 
 
contribution will cause a need for reduction of the applied external heat from the gas burner (or even in 
some cases a complete turn off!) to follow the ISO 834 standard temperature curve [49], see Figure 7.2. As 
a result, the combustible element is exposed to less external heat as opposed to a furnace test of a 
noncombustible structure. 
This does not reflect the conditions in a real fire scenario where the released energy from the combustible 
materials in the room will be the same regardless of the structures are of a combustible or noncombustible 
nature. The combustible structures are thus exposed to more heat than in the furnace test and the period 
of the load-bearing capacity could be significantly shorter than predicted. The charring rates and char depth 
based on a furnace test under completely different fire exposure conditions than a real fire could be on the 
unsafe side.  
 
Figure 7.2 Natural gas consumption recorded during furnace testing of noncombustible 
concrete slabs and combustible CLT-slabs [54]. 
This is a topic being widely discussed, but it still needs further investigation. Despite how interesting this 
might be, it is out of the scope of this thesis. But it underlines once again, that when designing buildings 
with combustible and exposed timber structures, the conditions should always be carefully considered. The 
author strongly encourages this field to be investigated further.  
 
 
==PAGE:37==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
25 
 
 
8 
Travelling fires 
As described in the previous chapters, there are well known models to describe and predict the fire 
propagation and time-temperature curves in small standard fire compartments often representing rooms in 
residential buildings. When designing structures for fire load either the standard fire exposure or a 
parametric fire exposure is used, see section 6.8. Each of these exposure curves defines a temperature at a 
given time. The standard curve is incrementally increasing with no decay or cooling phase. The parametric 
fire exposure is a full duration of the fire including the decay phase. Both models assume uniform 
conditions and a ‘well-stirred’ case for the post flashover scenario.  However, these models have limitations 
not only regarding compartment size but also material properties and fire load and cannot without further 
investigation be applied to large compartments as the fire dynamics may be very different which challenges 
these methods.  
One of the major challenges when investigating travelling fires is that one specific travelling fire does not 
exist. It is a family of fires and each compartment has its own worst-case scenario where the fire spread is 
unpredictable [55]. 
For a structural fire design the flashover scenario has always been the worst-case scenario. In a large 
compartment this could be different. Experiments carried out for travelling fires [56] challenges flashover 
fires as the sole worst-case scenario in designing buildings but further research, especially regarding 
travelling fires in compartments with exposed timber constructions, is still needed [13].  
Recent research known as the Improved Travelling Fire Methodology iTFM describes fire methodologies to 
design travelling fire scenarios in open-plan compartments [7]. However, these models do not take the 
presence of exposed timber into account and there is advice against using the methodology for fire design 
purposes in constructions with exposed mass timber structures [57]. 
8.1 
Characteristics  
When a localised fire initiates in a large compartment it only affects the nearby surroundings (near field), 
either by direct flame impingement or by heat radiation, while the structures further away from the fire 
(far field) experience less impact from fire and temperature. The hot smoke flowing along the ceiling can 
however affect the structures and combustible materials at the far end of the room. 
The initial fire will if not suppressed ignite the combustible fuel in the proximity which again leads to 
ignition of more unburnt combustible fuel. When the fuel at one location has burnt out, the fire and thus 
the flame front moves through the compartment igniting new fuel and leaving burnt out fuel behind. This 
behaviour is known as a ‘travelling fire’. The travelling fire has a leading edge (the flame front) and a trailing 
edge, see Figure 8.1. These edges move independently [55] and the distance between the edges define the 
size of the travelling fire. A travelling fire will not be the case in a small compartment as it needs enough 
space for this travelling behaviour to take place.  
This non-uniform burning differs from a fully developed fire, where all the combustible material in the 
compartment is assumed to be involved in the fire. In a travelling fire scenario, the temperatures 
throughout the compartment are inhomogeneous as opposed to the small compartment scenario, where 
homogenous temperatures are assumed.  
==PAGE:38==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
26 
 
 
 
 
Figure 8.2 Illustration of near field and far field temperatures. The temperature peaks at the location of the travelling fire 
(near field) [58]. 
8.2 
Fire spread 
The velocity of the travelling fire depends on many factors, e.g compartment geometries, fire origin, fire 
load, heat release rate of the fuel and the opening factor. If visible unprotected timber structures are 
present, the amount and location of the exposed timber also affects the behaviour of the travelling fire. 
This makes the prediction of the fire behaviour and fire dynamics under these conditions very complex. The 
fire spread is less influenced by the thermal properties of the lining materials [13].  
8.3 
Previous work and experiments 
In the literature travelling fires in compartments of 10 m x 50 m [59] and 10 m x 40 m [60] have been 
investigated using CFD-simulations. Full-scale experiments have been carried out in compartments of 5.6 m 
x 22.9 m [61] and of 10.8 m x 35.5 m x 3 m [5]. All these efforts were to investigate behaviours of travelling 
fires. However, the compartments were within the limits of the parametric fire regime described in the 
Eurocode [50], and all the compartments had structures of a noncombustible nature (steel or concrete). 
The fire load consisted of timber (wood cribs) distributed on the floor. Only a few experiments have been 
carried out in large compartments with exposed combustible linings and structures [62] [4]. 
In this thesis a large compartment exceeding the limits of parametric fire design according to Eurocode is 
considered. Furthermore, the structures and linings are made of partially exposed timber. In this case, the 
exposed timber contributes to the fire load in the compartment thus affecting the overall fire dynamics in 
the compartment. The CFD-simulations using the program Fire Dynamics Simulator (FDS) are described in 
the following chapter.  
Figure 8.1 Visual 
representation of a 
travelling fire with a 
leading edge and a trailing 
edge in a compartment 
with an exposed timber 
ceiling. The fire spreads 
along the floor igniting 
unburnt fuel and leaving 
burnt out fuel behind. The 
flames impinge on the 
combustible timber ceiling 
which ignites and 
combusts [57]. 
==PAGE:39==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
27 
 
 
9 
FDS-modelling 
The purpose of the FDS-modelling is to investigate how travelling fires behave in a large compartment with 
partially exposed mass timber constructions under different ventilation conditions. The compartment is 
modelled in FDS version 6.7.9. 
To investigate the field outside the well-known models based on smaller standard fire compartments, the 
model has a size exceeding the area of validity for the parametric fire, see section 6.8.2. The model also has 
a dimension where a travelling fire scenario is realistic. The model domain is with respect to the 
computational time not larger than needed. 
For FDS-input file see Appendix A: FDS-input file. 
9.1 
Model preconditions 
To set up a manageable FDS-model some reasonable boundary conditions and simplicities must be made. 
These are described in the following sections.  
9.1.1 
Delamination and char layer fall-off 
In the FDS-model delamination and char layer fall-off is not considered. It is important to note, that in a real 
fire design the risk of delamination and char layer fall-off should always be considered as this will lead to a 
renewed burning and increase of the heat release rate when virgin timber gets exposed. This is especially 
the case when CLT-members are being used and the thermal properties of the glue is unknown. 
9.1.2 
Charring rate 
The fire load and heat release rate contributions from the timber depends on the charring rate and vice 
versa. The charring rate depends on multiple factors, and it is rather complex to establish a precise 
estimate of the fuel contribution from the structural timber which must be calculated using iterations. This 
would be very complex, if not even impossible, to model in FDS. Furthermore, for non-uniform conditions 
the charring rate is not easily predicted as described in section 4.5.1 .  
The literature background review revealed a wide range of charring rates and charring depths depending 
on the fire and the location and type of timber. To use representative values the following assumptions are 
made. 
In the simulations we use constant values of the charring rate of β = 0.7 mm/min and β = 1 mm/min. When 
the char layer reaches a thickness of 40 mm the fire is assumed to cease due to the protective and 
insulating properties of the char. This corresponds to burning times of the exposed timber of 60 and 40 
minutes respectively.  
It is important to notice, that assuming a constant charring rate is an unrealistic assumption. However, to 
investigate the behaviour of the travelling fire on a qualitative level when exposed timber is introduced, 
compared to a scenario with no exposed timber present, a constant value of the charring is justified.  
9.1.3 
Self-extinction of timber structures 
It would be very complex to model self-extinction of the timber structures as this phenomenon is not yet 
fully understood, unpredictable and depends on many parameters, see section 4.3. Instead, we assume a 
constant charring rate and a maximum charring depth of 40 mm. At this point the char layer will act as an 
==PAGE:40==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
28 
 
 
insulation layer that slows down the charring and pyrolysis. Furthermore, with a cross section reduction of 
40 mm on each exposed side, together with the elevated temperatures and subsequently loss of strength 
and stiffness, the structures are assumed to be at their limit of the load-bearing capacity at this time. This 
means that in the simulations even though the fuel has completely burned out, the timber will continue to 
burn with the same HRR. As described in section 4.3, the HRR will most likely decrease when the fuel is 
burnt out. But since a reignition can occur, it is on the safe side to assume a constant HRR. In effect, this 
does not alter the results, as our scope is only to investigate the behaviour of the fire until all fuel is burnt 
out.  
9.1.4 
Openings 
All openings are assumed open from the beginning and throughout the duration of the fire. This is normally 
not the case in a real fire, where the openings in the beginning are closed or the glass might even never 
break. As described in section 6.2 this should always be assessed in a fire design, as the fire behaviour and 
temperature in the compartment is strongly dependent of the ventilation conditions.  
In this case the purpose is to investigate the influence of the opening factor on the fire propagation and this 
assumption is therefore valid in this simulation.  
9.1.5 
Initial fire 
The fire origin (initial fire) is fixed in all simulations to make the results comparable. The location is also 
chosen as it provides the best premises for investigated the travelling fire along the compartment. It also 
aligns with the previously mentioned experiments that were carried out in large compartments [4] [5]. 
Any change of the location of the initial fire would alter the results and in a real fire scenario the origin of 
the fire cannot be predicted and more locations should be considered. 
9.1.6 
Fire load density 
The fire load contribution from the exposed timber structures is added to the moveable fire load (from 
furniture and office equipment). In a fire design other permanent fire loads in the building e.g., combustible 
installations etc., should be added to the moveable fire load, see section 7. 
In these simulations we do not consider the fire load from permanent combustible installations etc. The 
purpose of the simulations is to investigate how travelling fires behave under different ventilation 
conditions and under different amounts and locations of exposed timber. Since the permanent fire load 
from installations etc. in all scenarios is equal, it is a valid assumption to disregard this.  
9.1.7 
Simultaneously ignition 
The fire load is modelled as cubic fuel packages with 5 exposed surfaces. When just one surface reaches a 
temperature of 300 °C, all 5 surfaces of the fuel package are assumed to ignite simultaneously. This is 
modelled to secure a well predicted fire load for each fuel package. The fuel package is of limited size 
(1 m x 1 m x 1 m) so it is a valid assumption to consider all surfaces to be ignited simultaneously. Local 
effects of openings and high air velocity could affect and cool one or more surfaces of the cube. Also, the 
surface facing the fire or hot smoke layer will experience higher temperatures than the other surfaces. This 
is not considered as it will not significantly affect the overall fire dynamics in the large compartment. 
==PAGE:41==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
29 
 
 
9.1.8 
Fire spread rate on surfaces 
When one of the surfaces of a fuel packages reaches 300 °C it ignites on all surfaces. When ignition starts, 
the fire is modelled to spread radially from the center of each side of the cube with a constant speed of  
v = 0.01 m/s. In reality the flame spread velocity depends on multiple factors, e.g the type of material, 
thickness, imposed heat flux, upwards or downwards flame spread etc. The exact flame spread on the fuel 
package surfaces would get too complex to model and this level of detail would not correspond with the 
overall purpose of the scope of the model. However, a certain flame spread must be provided since the 
whole surface will not burn instantly. With the assigned flame spread of v = 0.01 m/s the maximum HRR is 
reached with the whole surface burning after 70 s.  
Due to uniform temperatures of the walls (at the same height) and ceiling no fire spread rates are applied 
on the exposed timber and ceiling surfaces and they are assumed to ignite simultaneously. This assumption 
is validated in section 9.16 and 9.17. 
9.1.9 
Fire safety installations and fire brigade intervention 
Depending on the type of occupancy and size of compartment the national building codes prescribes 
demands for technical fire safety installations such as an automatic fire ventilation or an automatic 
sprinkler system. These fire safety installations are required to secure safe egress routes and to control and 
prevent spreading of the fire. 
In the simulation no fire safety installations or fire brigade intervention are considered as the purpose is to 
investigate the propagation and behaviour of an unsuppressed travelling fire.  
9.2 
Model compartment size 
We are interested in investigating a travelling fire in a large compartment that exceeds the limitations of a 
standard fire compartment with the limits of Af = 500 m² and H = 4 m, see section 6.8.2. 
A compartment of 5 m x 15 m x 45 m (Af = 675 m2 > 500 m2, H = 5 m > 4 m) is analysed. This long and 
relatively narrow compartment is, depending on the ventilation conditions, considered valid for 
investigating travelling fire scenarios.  
9.3 
Model domain 
To proper simulate the flow through the openings, the model domain includes outside spaces alongside the 
compartment. These wide spaces are fully open on all sides and each side (except the ground level) is 
modelled in FDS with the command &VENT and SURF_ID=‘OPEN’. 
A width of the outside spaces of 5 m on each side of the openings is considered adequate to ensure the 
flow through the openings does not get affected by domain limitations.  
The total model domain has a size of 5 m x 25 m x 45 m.  
9.4 
Openings to the outside 
The openings between the fire compartment and the outside spaces are modelled in FDS with the 
command ‘&HOLE’ in the side walls (FDS obstacles). To ensure symmetrical conditions for the travelling 
fire, each side along the x-axis is provided with openings, see Figure 9.1. 
==PAGE:42==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
30 
 
 
 
 
Figure 9.1 Model domain with compartment with 154 fuel packages and fully open spaces outside to account for 
flow through openings. Openings on each side (symmetrical conditions). (Concrete ceiling and left end wall not 
shown). Initial fire position is fixed in the middle of the first row in all scenarios. Left: Model with opening factor 
O=0.05 m2. Right: Model with opening factor O=0.10 m½.  
 
9.5 
Compartment boundaries 
All boundaries (ceiling, end walls, side walls and floor) of the compartment are modelled as concrete with 
thickness 30 cm. In the simulations where exposed timber is present, a part of the concrete boundary (wall 
or ceiling) is substituted with timber. I.e., the ceiling or the walls will in some of the simulations consist 
partly of concrete and partly of timber depending on the area of exposed timber. To assign physical and 
thermal properties to the boundaries, they are modelled with the command &VENT for external 
boundaries (ceiling and end walls) and the command &OBST for boundaries (side walls) inside the model 
domain.  
9.5.1 
Thermal properties 
The thermal properties of the boundaries influence the gas temperature and fire propagation in the 
compartment as a higher thermal inertia leads to a lower gas temperature. Boundaries with high thermal 
inertia are capable of absorbing heat thus lowering the temperature in the compartment. Since the thermal 
properties of the boundaries affect the gas temperature in the compartment it is essential that correct 
values are used. The thermal inertia varies with temperature, see Table 14.1 in Appendix B: Thermal 
properties of wood and concrete. 
The variation of the thermal properties is modelled in FDS with &RAMP-function with values of “k” and “cp” 
at given temperatures. It must be noted that the density also varies with temperature, especially for wood 
when it transforms to a char with low density. This is however not possible to model in FDS. It could be on 
the unsafe side since the thermal inertia is over-estimated.  
9.6 
Submeshes 
To save computational time, the domain is divided into submeshes.  The number of submeshes must be 
dividable with the number of blocks used in the simulation. The fire compartment is divided into 3x11 
submeshes and the outside spaces are divided into 11 and 12 submeshes, giving a total of 56 submeshes, 
dividable by 4 blocks, see Figure 9.2. 
Z 
x 
y 
Position of initial 
fire FP1.4 
==PAGE:43==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
31 
 
 
The mesh boundaries must not cut through fuel packages or openings as this will affect the accuracy of the 
simulation. For the same reason all submeshes have a height of 5 m corresponding to the height of the 
domain.  
It is desirable not to have any submeshes cut through locations with complicated phenomena and fire, if 
possible [63]. In this case, with a travelling fire moving through the compartment, it is not possible to 
isolate the fire in one submesh.  
 
Figure 9.2 Model domain divided in 56 submeshes.  
9.7 
Material properties 
In the FDS-simulation we assign each material with the corresponding thermal properties. As mentioned in 
section 9.5.1 the material properties of wood and concrete are temperature dependant, see Table 14.1 in 
Appendix B: Thermal properties of wood and concrete The fuel is wood and is also assigned with properties 
for combustion, soot yield and molecular formula, see Table 9.1 and Table 14.1.

Radiation 
To account for radiation from surfaces, flames and smoke the FDS file is supplied with the command below.

interested in investigating temperatures of boundaries [66].

9.9 
Output commands, slices and devices 
To monitor and investigate the results of the simulations, the FDS-input files are supplied with the following 
commands for output data. The command lines are only given as examples. For the complete FDS-input 
command lines, please see the FDS-input files in Appendix A: FDS-input file. 
9.9.1 
Wall temperatures 
&BNDF QUANTITY = 'WALL TEMPERATURE'/ 
This command yields the temperature of all boundaries and obstacles in the compartment. In Smokeview it 
is presented as a visualisation with qualitative colouring, but it is also possible to extract exact data using 
the fds2ascii-command in the DOS-prompt [65].  
9.9.2 
Oxygen volume fraction 
To investigate whether the oxygen fraction has an impact on the fire behaviour, slices cutting through the 
compartment in the x- and y direction have been assigned with the command:   
&SLCF ID='OXYGEN X=4', PBX=4, QUANTITY='VOLUME FRACTION',SPEC_ID='OXYGEN'/ 
==PAGE:45==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
33 
 
 
Oxygen is already predefined in FDS, so no further needs to be assigned.  
9.9.3 
Gas temperature 
Investigating time-temperature curves is a basic discipline for both sensitivity purposes and for the 
discussion of the simulations. Temperature- and thermocouple devices are applied measuring the gas 
temperature in the compartment at different spatial coordinates. The temperature detectors are supplied 
with thermocouples as they could provide a more stable output.  
&DEVC ID='TEMP_x=2.50 y=2.50 z=2.0', XYZ=2.50, 2.50, 2.0, QUANTITY='TEMPERATURE'/ 
&DEVC ID='TC_x=2.50 y=2.50 z=2.0', XYZ=2.50, 2.50, 2.0, QUANTITY='THERMOCOUPLE'/ 
To get a quick visualization of the temperatures the devices are supplied with either vertical or horizontal 
slices cutting through the compartment. 
&SLCF ID='Y=0.5', PBY = 0.5, QUANTITY='TEMPERATURE' / 
9.9.4 
Mass and volume flow 
To investigate the flow through openings FDS-commands are inserted in the file to extract these data. The 
flow is of special interest when examining the fire dynamics in the simulations with exposed timber 
present. 
&DEVC C ID='DOOR 1.1_mass_flow_1', XB=9.0,12.0, 0.0,0.0, 0.0,1.0, QUANTITY='MASS FLUX Y', 
SPATIAL_STATISTIC='AREA INTEGRAL' / 
&DEVC ID='DOOR 1.1_vol_flow_1', XB=9.0,12.0, 0.0,0.0, 0.0,1.0, QUANTITY ='V-VELOCITY', 
SPATIAL_STATISTIC='AREA INTEGRAL' / 
These commands lines list data for the mass and volume flow in the y-direction through an opening (3 m 
wide door) located at the boundary with y = 0 m. The examples are for 0 m < z < 1 m. 
9.10 
Preliminary studies 
To investigate a travelling fire, we desire a fuel-controlled fire propagating through the compartment. To 
ensure this we need to design our openings large enough.  
If the smoke layer builds up too quickly the combustible material in the compartment including the 
exposed timber, will ignite before the leading edge of the travelling fire reaches the fuel. This flashover 
scenario is not desired when we investigate travelling fires.  
Some initial test runs of the model were carried out to “tune” the model. It was very clear that an opening 
factor below 0.05 m½ was not appropriate for this case as it quickly led to a ventilation-controlled fire and 
flashover scenario with flames outside the openings, meaning no combustion can take place under these 
conditions inside the compartment. Therefore, only opening factors O ≥ 0.05 m½ are considered in this 
simulation.  
To ensure we get a travelling fire we also need to control the fire load. If not, the fire evolves very rapidly 
with high temperatures throughout the compartment with a flashover scenario. This fire scenario could be 
a realistic case, but not suited to investigate the behaviour of a travelling fire. During the preliminary 
==PAGE:46==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
34 
 
 
studies of the model the maximum HRR, the total fire load Q and the ventilation conditions were altered to 
find the optimal conditions for the model.  
Please refer to Appendix C: Preliminary studies for a full description of the preliminary studies. 
9.10.1 Conclusion of the preliminary studies 
Based on the multiple preliminary simulations described in Appendix C: Preliminary studies it was 
concluded, that a HRRPUA = 300 kW/m² and a fire load of QFP = 1,315 MJ pr. fuel package provided 
conditions for a travelling fire to take place in this size of compartment. In the final FDS-simulations these 
values are fixed for all simulations. The opening factors are O=0.05 m½ and O=0.10 m½.  
The fire load density was chosen as q = 300 MJ/m2 (floor area) which is representative for a large variety of 
occupancies e.g., stores, manufacturing and industrial plants [67].

9.11.3 Notation 
Each fuel package is referred to as FPa.b, where “a” indicates the row number in the x-direction and “b” 
indicates the row number in the y-direction. I.e., FP4.5 is the fuel package placed in the 4th row in the x-
direction and 5th row in the y-direction. 
On each side of the five visible sides of the fuel package a vent is modelled with properties for wood, 
spread rate 0.01 m/s and a burner with HRRPUA=300 kW/m2 and a RAMP_Q function designed as the HRR-
curve illustrated in Figure 9.4. All 5 surfaces of the wooden cube are assumed to simultaneously ignite 
when just one of the surfaces reaches a maximum temperature of 300 °C, which is the onset temperature 
for the pyrolysis, see section 4.2.  
To identify the temperatures of each surface input files each surface is notated as listed in Table 9.2. 
For the FDS command lines, please see Appendix A: FDS-input file. Surface z1 is facing the ground and thus 
not supplied with a vent.
