9.13 
Sensitivity analysis 
This sensitivity analysis is carried out to investigate how the change of one (or more) input parameters 
affects the output. If a minor variation of an input parameter has a high impact on the result (output) of the 
model, the model is sensitive to a change of this specific parameter. This should be assessed when 
analysing the results as all input parameters are subject to a degree of uncertainty. On the other hand, if 
even a major variation of an input parameter only affects the output of the model at a minor scale, the 
model is resilient to a change of this specific parameter. The latter leading to the conclusion that the output 
based on the specific input is reliable, as a variation would give approximately the same result.  
One of the basic sensitivity analyses for an FDS-model is determining the mesh resolution. This is discussed 
in the following section.  
9.13.1 Optimal mesh resolution  
To set up a reliable model we need to find the optimal mesh. An optimal mesh is where each cell is small 
enough to give valid results but no smaller than it justifies the extra computational time.  Valid results are 
reached when the output values (HRR, temperature etc.) within a specified criteria converge with 
increasing number of cells i.e., the results do not change significantly with a finer mesh.  
9.13.1.1 Characteristic diameter D* 
To determine the optimal mesh we use the characteristic diameter D* [65] for buoyant plumes. In the 
simulation a various number of fuel packages are burning simultaneously, starting with just one fuel 
package (the initial fire). The heat release rate increases as more and more fuel packages get involved until 
stable conditions or a fully developed fire occurs.  
Considering one fuel package with HRRPUA = 300 kW/m² pr. surface, HRRFP = 5 m²*300 kW/m² = 1,500 kW 
we find the characteristic diameter   
𝐷∗= (
1,500
1,100)
2
5 = 1.1 m 
For a row of 7 fuel packages burning simultaneously we find HRR = 7*1,500 kW = 10,500 kW and the 
corresponding characteristic diameter   
𝐷∗= (10,500
1,100 )

5 = 2,5 m 
Mesh resolution with cell size δx should be within the range of [46]

==PAGE:51==

When determining the optimal mesh the size of burner surface should also be considered providing a high 
enough resolution. The discretization of the burner should be less than [46]  
𝛿𝑥< √𝐴𝑏
8 = √1 𝑚2
8
= 0.35 𝑚  
Ab is the area of the burner (one side of the fuel package) 
It is important to notice that the above discretization is based on the HRR of the fire. Local conditions could 
lead to other criteria for the optimal mesh with either a coarser or a finer mesh.  
In all FDS-simulations the same grid discretization is used throughout the model domain. The 
computational time could have been reduced with a coarser mesh in some areas. However, different grid 
cells sizes could lead to loss of information when flow leaves a coarser mesh and enters a finer mesh [71]. 
With the same grid discretization throughout the domain this error source is eliminated. Furthermore, the 
fire travels along the compartment. It is not possible to “isolate” the fire in some area with a finer grid. The 
grid must be fine enough to ensure an accurate model of the fire and fire spread. 
The limits based on the above assumptions provide a wide range of possible mesh resolutions. In the 
following, mesh resolutions with δx of 0.100 m, 0.125 m, 0.200 m and 0.250 m are investigated.  
Number of cells shall always be an integer divisor for the X, Y and Z-direction (IJK) for the mesh. Also, the 
cell size shall be equal to or smaller than the thickness of the walls and any obstacles to include them in the 
simulation. The pressure solver in FDS uses Fast Fourier transformations and JK must be integers giving by 
the expression 2𝑙∙3𝑚∙5𝑛 with l, m and n as integers [46] [65].  
The total model domain is 5 x 25 x 45 m. The fire compartment is 5 x 15 x 45 m. See Table 9.3 for mesh 
resolution and cell numbers. All cells are cubic with same side length. All J and K-numbers comply with the 
Fast Fourier integers.

9.13.1.2 Results and discussion 
In Figure 9.6 and Figure 9.7 the HRR-curves for different cell sizes (δx) are illustrated for opening factors 
O=0.05 m½ and O=0.10 m½.  
At the initial and growth stage of the fire the influence of the cell size is less significant as the ventilation 
conditions and fire dynamics during this period is less predominant. The fire can be considered “free 
==PAGE:52==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
40 
 
 
burning” at this stage, where the compartment conditions have low influence on the fire, and the HRR only 
depends on the actual fuel properties and heat of combustion.  
A coarse mesh with δx = 0.250 m does not provide conditions to simulate a proper fire spread. Even though 
the fire reaches a HRR of 10 MW at t=30 min, and a mesh with 0.250 m cells is within the limits (see section 
9.13.1.1), it must be concluded that this resolution is to coarse for a fire spread to take place leaving 
unburnt fuel packages, see Figure 9.8. The discretization of the burner surface is 4x4 cells. This 
discretization is too coarse and not suited to simulate fire spread from one fuel package to the next. The 
higher opening factor, the more sensitive the model, see Figure 9.9. The HRR is underpredicted with a 
factor 2 at t = 30 min compared to the fine meshes. 
 
Figure 9.6 HRR-curves for different cell sizes dx and opening 
factor O=0.05 m½.  
 
Figure 9.7 HRR-curves for different cell sizes dx and opening 
factor O=0.10 m½.  
 
Figure 9.8 End of simulation with O=0.05 m½ and δx = 0.250 m. 
The fire spread stops at row 5 leaving numerous unburnt fuel 
packages. 
 
Figure 9.9 End of simulation with O=0.10 m½ and δx = 0.250 m. 
The fire spread stops at row 2 leaving numerous unburnt fuel 
packages. The higher opening factor leads to cooling of the 
compartment and more flow through openings. The cells are 
too coarse to support fire spread and the fire ceases. 
 
The medium and fine meshes (δX < 0.250 m) provide conditions for the fire spread to take place for both 
opening factor 0.05 m½ and 0.10 m½  involving all the fuel in the fire as illustrated in Figure 9.6 and Figure 
9.7. However, the mesh with δx = 0.200 m is more sensitive to ventilation conditions giving an unstable and 
fluctuating HRR-output. The higher opening factor the more unstable conditions, see Figure 9.10. Each 
burner surface has in this case 25 (5x5) cells. The influence of the ventilation conditions limits and 
overwhelms the potential maximum HRR of the burner, leading to a lower HRR and a longer fire duration 
compared to the finer meshes. The impact of the opening factor and ventilation conditions is evident, and 
this mesh is also considered too coarse.

ventilation conditions until flashover, see Figure 9.13 and Figure 9.14. The finer mesh with 0.100 m cells 
overestimates the HRR with 10 – 15 % compared to the mesh with 0.125 m cells.  
With the lower opening factor of 0.05 m½ less energy and hot gases can flow out of the openings and less 
cool air can entrain. This leads to higher temperatures in the compartment and flashover takes place about 
10 – 15 minutes before the scenario with an opening factor of 0.10 m½.

The HRR increases until t = 30 min and then decreases before slowly rising again. This I not due to lack of 
oxygen (ventilation-controlled fire) but because in the growth phase the boundaries are affecting the fire 
giving ground for more heat to build and thus igniting more fuel packages. In more general terms there is a 
“hot enclosure” at the end of the compartment where the heat radiates from both the ceiling, end wall and 
side walls. As the fire progresses the openings will affect the temperature and HRR.  
9.13.1.3 Convergence analysis 
In Figure 9.15 the HRR-output at given times is plotted to investigate the convergence and find the optimal 
mesh. The HRR is plotted against the reciprocal value of the cubic cell size 1/δx [m½] to make it independent 
of the absolute number of cells in the compartment. 
Note: In the FDS-guide [65] plotting against D*/δx is recommended. In this case where D* is a function of 
HRR this is not a useable method.  
The HRR-output converges nicely for increasing 1/ δx (smaller cell sizes) during the first period of the fire 
(t < 17 min) where the burning is less influenced by the fire dynamics and the ventilation conditions. This 
corresponds to the HRR-curves illustrated in Figure 9.6. As the fire evolves the difference of the HRR output 
is more significant and convergence is less predominant. The difference between 1/ δx = 8 (δx =0.125 m) 
and 1/ δx = 10 (δx = 0.100 m) under stable conditions before flashover (t=130 min) is 10 – 20 %.

9.13.2 Sensitivity analysis for baseline model 
To investigate the sensitivity of the model further we change some of the input parameters. As baseline 
model we use the model with opening factor O=0.05 m½, HRRPUA=300 kW/m², QFP=1,315 MJ, and  
δx = 0.125 m. 
The sensitivity for the HRR- and temperature output is investigated by  
1. increasing the opening factor from 0.05 m½ to 0.10 m½  
2. refining the mesh by changing the cell size from 0.125 m to 0.100 m  
3. making a coarser mesh by changing the cell size from 0.125 m to 0.200 m  
4. increasing the fire load of one fuel package QFP with 20 % from QFP =1,315 MJ to QFP =1,578 MJ

9.13.2.1 Opening factor 
As illustrated in Figure 9.19 increasing the opening factor from base model with O=0.05 m½ to O=0.10 m½ 
only affects the HRR at a small scale (less than 10 %) until late in the fire (120 min < t ), where flashover 
occurs. This is also illustrated in Figure 9.13.  
In the growth phase the fuel properties and heat of combustion will be the determining factors of the fire 
propagation and HRR. The fire is fuel-controlled until flashover occurs. This means, increasing the opening 
factor does not affect the HRR until flashover as the full potential of the fire is already reached giving 
almost the same fire spread, see Figure 9.22. With the fine cell of 0.125 m the ventilation conditions do not 
affect the stability of the HRR as described in section 9.13.1.2. This means that the model is resilient to 
changes of the HRR when the opening factor is doubled for the fuel-controlled fire.  
Note: A decrease in the opening factor could significantly alter the HRR, as the fire gets ventilation-
controlled. 
Figure 9.22 Upper left: Fire spread at t=33 min for O=0.05 m½. Upper right: Fire spread at t=33 min for O=0.10 m½. Lower left: Fire 
spread at t=50 min for O=0.05 m½. Lower right: Fire spread at t=50 min for O=0.10 m½. The fire spread is similar for both opening 
factors at the same time giving same HRR, but the higher opening factor releases more hot gases and limits the transverse fire 
spread near the openings.

Increasing the opening factor from 0.05 m½ to 0.10 m½ lowers the temperatures in the compartment by  
20 – 30 %, see Figure 9.20. The higher opening factor allows for more air to enter and cooling of the 
compartment as well as allowing more hot smoke to leave the compartment through the openings. The 
deviation increases as the fire travels and the openings have more impact on the fire dynamics allowing 
even more hot gases to be released and more cold air to entrain.  
In Figure 9.23 the time-temperature curves are illustrated for opening factors O=0.05 m½ and O=0.10 m½.  
 
 
It is noticeable that in this large compartment a higher opening factor leads to lower temperatures. This is 
opposite the case in a standard fire compartment (parametric fire) where an increase in the opening factor 
leads to higher temperatures as more combustion can take place due to the higher oxygen level, see Figure 
6.4Figure 9.23. As stated previously in section 6.8.2 the parametric fire curves do not apply for large 
compartments which this output confirms.  
9.13.2.2 Cell size 
From Figure 9.19 it is clear that the HRR-output at given times depends on the cell size. A finer mesh gives 
higher HRR-output values and a coarser mesh gives lower HRR-values. The total energy release (area under 
the HRR-curve) is the same regardless the cell size.  
The model domain is divided into 56 submeshes as described in section 9.6. Information must be 
transferred properly from submesh to submesh. The finer the mesh, the more accurate information 
transfer and refined data. The finer mesh is more sensitive to fire spread and consequently more fuel 
packages get ignited than with the coarse mesh at the same time of the simulation. More fuel packages 
burning simultaneously lead to higher HRR and higher temperatures, see Figure 9.17 and Figure 9.18.  
With the coarser cell of δx = 0.200 m the fire spread is less progressed as illustrated in Figure 9.11 and 
Figure 9.12 with fewer fuel packages burning. This indicates that the mesh does not support an accurate 
fire spread and underpredicts the HRR as well as the temperature.  
The finer mesh predicts the HRR and temperatures 5-20 % above the base model. The coarser mesh 
underpredicts the HRR by 20-40 % and the temperatures by 10-40 % compared to the base model.

9.13.2.3 Fire load 
The fire load of each fuel package was increased by 20 %. This also increased the total duration of the fire 
for one fuel package from 23 min s to 27 min, as the HRRmax = 300 kW/m² was not altered and it takes more 
time to consume the higher amount of fuel.  
The progress (travel speed) of the fire does not change with increased fire load. But the longer fire duration 
produces more energy and increases the temperature in the compartment, see Figure 9.21. 
The far end of the compartment (25 m < x) experiences less impact and the temperature rise is 5 – 20 %.  
At 0 m < x < 15 m the temperature deviation is above 30 %. At this part of the compartment the impact 
from the boundaries affect the temperature rise as there are no openings here for the case with opening 
factor 0.05 m½.  
When the fire travels the temperature deviation decreases (t= 67 min), but later (t=83 min) the deviation 
increases due to more hot smoke being produced. 
 
Figure 9.24 Upper left: Fire spread at t=33 min for O=0.05 m½. Upper right: Fire spread at t=33 min for Q increased with 20 %.  
Lower left: Fire spread at t=50 min for O=0.05 m½. Lower right: Fire spread at t=50 min for Q increased with 20 %. The longer fire 
duration with increased fuel leads to more simultaneously burning fuel packages. The leading edge of the fire is not affected.  
 
9.13.3 Sensitivity analysis - Conclusion 
Sensitivity studies were carried out for 4 different mesh resolutions having cell sizes δx of 0.250 m, 0.200 m, 
0.125 m and 0.100 m. Each model had an opening factor of either O=0.05 m½ or O=0.10 m½ giving a total of 
4x2 = 8 models. Based on these studies the optimal mesh resolution was found to have a cell size  
δx = 0.125 m. This mesh resolution provides reliable data and is only slightly affected by the ventilation 
conditions of the compartment.  
The sensitivity of the base model with opening factor O=0.05 m½, HRRPUA=300 kW/m², QFP=1,315 MJ, and 
δx = 0.125 m was investigated by changing the following input parameters: Cell size, opening factor and fuel 
load. The sensitivity analysis demonstrated that the base model is resilient for changes in the opening 
factor from O=0.05 m½ to O=0.10 m½ regarding the heat release rate, as the HRR is not significantly affected 
by the opening factor during stable conditions and fuel-controlled conditions.  
==PAGE:61==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
49 
 
 
Using a finer mesh with δx = 0.100 m results in a faster fire spread with higher HRR and higher temperatures 
and a 15-minute shorter fire duration. The base model underestimates the temperatures with 5-10 % 
compared to the finer mesh with cells of δx = 0.100 m.  
Increasing the opening factor by a factor 2 lowers the temperatures in the compartment by 20-30 % 
depending on the location and time. The longer the fire has progressed, the higher the impact of the 
opening factor on the temperature.  
Increasing the fuel load by 20 % did not impact the fire spread rate but increased the temperatures by  
5 – 35 %. The largest deviations were found at the part of the compartment where the initial fire started 
with no nearby openings.  
When analyzing the results of the FDS-simulations, see section 9.14, considerations should be made when 
the temperature output is close to limits where a cell size of 0.100 m could alter the results significantly.  
This will be discussed further in section 9.16. 
The following FDS-simulations are only carried out for opening factors O=0.05 m½ and O=0.10 m½. As the 
opening factor affects the temperature, the temperatures for other scenarios could be either higher or 
lower, depending on the actual opening factor. As stated previously in the preliminary studies, see section 
9.10, the opening factor also has a high impact on the fire spread, especially for a low opening factor.  
As the purpose of this thesis is to investigate the behaviour of travelling fires and compare different models 
with or without exposed timber, the mesh resolution of 0.125 m is considered adequate. The extra 
computational time from 122 hours to 360 hours is not justified. Furthermore, the extra computational 
time would severely limit the number of scenarios that could be carried out within the time frame of this 
thesis. The following simulations (unless otherwise stated) will be with a mesh resolution of δx = 0.125 m. 
9.14 
Overview of scenarios 
To provide an adequate number of scenarios the following factors are considered: 
• 
Openings to the outside (opening factor) 
• 
Contribution to the fire load from the timber  
• 
Heat release rate of the timber 
• 
Location of the timber 
• 
Surface area of timber 
The cell size (mesh resolution) influences the output as discussed in section 9.13. 
Multiple scenarios are investigated to see how each parameter influences the behaviour of the travelling 
fire, see Table 9.5 . The baseline scenarios B1.1, B1.2, B2.1 and B2.2 have no exposed timber and are used 
as benchmarks. All simulations are carried out with mesh resolution δx = 0.125 m unless otherwise stated.

9.15.1.2 Travel speed 
Doubling the opening factor from 0.05 m½ to 0.10 m½ has no impact on the travel speed of the travelling 
fire. However, the openings affect the propagation of the fire makes the fire more “arrowlike” due to the 
cooling of the outer fuel packages, see Figure 9.25 and Figure 9.26 which increases the width (distance 
between the leading and trailing edge). 
The speed is 0.2 – 0.4 m/min in both scenarios, see Figure 9.27 and Figure 9.28. It takes approximately 140 
minutes to reach the far end of the compartment giving an average travel speed of 0.3 m/min. In real fire 
scenarios spread rates of 36 mm/s (2 m/min) has been reported [72]. These reports however do not 
disclose the actual conditions of the buildings which could be very different from this compartment with 
respect to both fuel load and geometry.  
 
==PAGE:64==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
52 
 
 
 
Figure 9.27 Scenario B1.1 Positions of leading and trailing edges. 
Both edges travel with same speed until flashover, where the 
leading edge accelerates.  
 
Figure 9.28 Scenario B1.2 Positions of leading and trailing edges. 
Both edges travel with same speed until flashover, where the 
leading edge accelerates. The travelling fire is wider (distance 
between leading and trailing edge) than in scenario B1.1. 
 
 
9.15.1.3 HRR-curves 
The HRR- and time-temperature curves were discussed in the sensitivity analysis in section 9.13 but are 
repeated in this section for a better overview. The HRR-curves are presented in Figure 9.30. The curves 
coincide until flashover indicating the fire is fuel-controlled in both cases. With an opening factor of  
0.05 m½ the overall temperature in the compartment increases, a flashover scenario takes place at an 
earlier stage and the fire duration decrease with 14 minutes (8 %) compared to the scenario with opening 
factor 0.10 m½.  
The maximum HRR increases with 23 % for B1.1 compared to B1.2. With a high opening factor, the fire is 
kept fuel-controlled longer and thus more fuel can burn before the temperature reaches a level where 
flashover scenario takes over and the fire gets ventilation-controlled. At this point there is less fuel left in 
the compartment since more fuel already has burn out. Hence, the HRR cannot reach the same level as

This behaviour is opposite a standard compartment fire where lowering the opening factor leads to a 
longer fire duration and lower HRR due to the fire being more ventilation-controlled. In this large 
compartment under these ventilation conditions the fire is fuel-controlled until flashover.  
9.15.1.4 Time-temperature curves 
The time-temperature curves for the center of the compartment (x,y,z)=(22.5 m, 7.5 m, 3.0 m) are 
illustrated in Figure 9.31. In scenario B1.1. the temperatures are up to 50 % higher than in scenario B1.2 
before flashover. As the travelling fire moves toward the center of the compartment, the temperature rises 
and peaks when the travelling fire is at the center. As the fire travels on, the temperature decreases but 
remains at a higher level due to flashover with elevated temperatures until the cooling phase.   
 
Figure 9.30 HHR-curves are coinciding (fuel-controlled fire) until 
flashover (ventilation-controlled). The lower opening factor leads 
to a flashover at an earlier stage due to higher temperatures.  
 
Figure 9.31 Time-temperature curves at center of fire 
compartment at (x,y,z)=(22.5 m, 7.5 m, 3.0 m). Lower opening 
factor leads to a 50 % increase in temperatures before flashover. 
Before flashover at t = 135 min 
The gas temperature in the compartment is non-uniform, see Figure 9.32, with temperature peaks 
following the location of the travelling fire. The maximum temperature above the localised fire is twice as 
high as the minimum temperature at the far end of the compartment. As the fire progresses, the difference 
between the temperatures at the initial fire end (x=2.5 m) and the far end (x=42.5 m) of the compartment 
decreases. Smoke and heat fill the compartment giving more homogenous conditions and the impact of the 
heated boundaries also increases the gas temperature.  
At and after flashover at t = 135 min 
The temperature accelerates reaching a maximum temperature of 1,000 °C. At this point there is only fuel 
left at the end of the compartment and the rest of the fuel has already burnt out. The remaining fuel ignites 
simultaneously. This explains the steep gradient and the temperature behind the trailing edge of the fire is 
homogeneous around 400 °C.

9.15.1.5 Baseline scenarios B1.1. and B1.2 - Conclusion 
The baseline scenarios B1.1 and B1.2 investigates the behaviour of the travelling fire in the compartment 
with an opening factor of 0.05 m½ and 0.10 m½ respectively and no exposed timber. The opening factor 
does not affect the travel speed of the travelling fire, but the size of the fire changes as the cooling effect of 
the openings affects the propagation of the fire transverse to the travelling direction. The fire is in both 
scenarios fuel-controlled until flashover. The lower opening factor leads to increased temperatures with 
50 % before flashover and a shorter fire duration. The temperatures in the compartment are non-uniform 
with high near-field temperatures and lower far-field temperatures - also after flashover. This non-uniform 
behaviour collides with the basic assumption of homogenous temperatures in a compartment.  
9.15.2 Baseline scenarios B2.1 and B2.2 
These baseline scenarios B2.1 and B2.2 are similar to baseline scenarios B1.1 and B1.2 respectively, see 
Table 9.5. The only change is the cell size δx that is refined to 0.100 m. These baselines scenarios only serve 
as benchmarks for the scenarios C2.1 and C2.2. Please refer to section 9.16.2.  
9.16 
Exposed timber ceiling 
In these scenarios parts of the ceiling are substituted with exposed timber, see Table 9.5. The area of the 
exposed timber is stated with respect to the total area of the walls and ceilings of the compartment 
(1,250 m²). In the Danish regulations [41], see section 5, the amount of exposed timber allowed is with 
respect to this area. To make it comparable, the same reference area is used here.  
The rest of the ceiling is kept as concrete. Partly to make it comparable to the baseline models with 
concrete ceiling and partly to consider, that in a large compartment like this with large spans, there must 
be some load-bearing elements to support the ceiling. These would often be concrete beams supported on 
concrete columns. In a ceiling construction e.g., CLT-slabs with partially exposed surfaces, the rest of the 
surface is often protected with gypsum boards or a similar fire protective cladding. The thermal inertia of 
the ceiling construction will affect the temperature. A concrete ceiling will, all other parts equal, lower the 
temperature in the compartment due to high thermal inertia compared to a gypsum ceiling. The actual

conditions of the fire compartment should therefore always be assessed and results from these simulations 
cannot without further investigation be applied to other compartments.  
9.16.1 FDS-modelling 
Each exposed timber square is modelled with the command &VENT. When just one point in the square 
reaches 300 °C, the whole surface of the square starts to burn. This is a valid assumption given the 
temperature at the ceiling within the limited areas is homogenous.  
The burner of each vent is modelled as described in section 0 with charring rate β=0,7 mm/min and a total 
duration of 60 minutes corresponding to a ~40 mm depth of char layer and a HRRPUA = 100 kW/m². 
Note: When substituting parts of the concrete ceiling with timber, firstly the timber areas are specified with 
command line &VENT and XB-coordinates. When all the timber are in place, the whole ceiling is modelled as 
concrete with &VENT overlapping the whole ceiling. As only one vent can be assigned to any surface, this 
puts out a warning in FDS telling us, that the concrete vents overlapping the timber vents are rejected.  This 
is precisely what we want to obtain, so the warning can be neglected. With this method just one command 
line for the concrete ceiling is necessary. To avoid the warning, all the areas on the ceiling between the 
timber areas could instead have been individually modelled resulting in multiple &VENT and XB-lines giving 
the same result but much more time consuming.  
9.16.2 Scenarios C1.1 and C1.2 
In these scenarios 10 % of the reference area (the total surface area of walls and ceiling of the 
compartment) is substituted with timber. To get a nice integer and make is suited to fit the compartment 
geometry, the area of the exposed timber is rounded up to 132 m². See Table 9.5 for data. 
The exposed timber is evenly distributed on the ceiling in squares of 2 m x 2 m, see Figure 9.33. Each 
square of timber is located inside one submesh, see section 9.6, to ensure the square does not overlap 
meshes as this could disturb the data transfer during the simulation.  
Note: Data for boundaries and slices mentioned in this section were extracted using the fds2ascii DOS-
command, please refer to FDS User’s Guide [65].  
9.16.2.1 Fire load 
The total amount of fire load of the timber is

ceiling in the compartment.   
9.16.2.2 Scenario C1.1 – Results and discussion 
Fire spread at ceiling 
In this scenario all the timber squares got ignited. The first ignition at the ceiling took place 24 minutes 
after the initial fire started at the square located immediately above the initial fire. The fire spread rate 
along the ceiling had an average velocity of 5.3 mm/min and the final square ignited 41 minutes after the 
initial fire start and 17 minutes after the first square ignited, see Figure 9.34.    
 
Figure 9.34 Scenario C1.1. Ignition times and temperature visualization of timber squares at 
ceiling at t=27 min. The timber squares have homogenous temperatures within each square 
with low standard deviation. The temperature of the timber is higher than the concrete 
ceiling with higher thermal inertia as the difference in the coloring indicates. The highlighted 
timber squares have been ignited and are burning.  
 
Timber T=228 °C 
Concrete T= 126 °C 
Ignition t=41 min 
Ignition t = 24 min 
==PAGE:69==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
57 
 
 
 
Figure 9.35 Scenario C1.1. Fire spread rate at ceiling. Spread rate 
declines at the end of the compartment due to locally reduced oxygen 
volume fraction and temperature.  
The spread rate declines at x=34 m, see Figure 9.35 which delays the ignition of the squares at the far end.  
The far end (opposite the initial fire) is colder due to the longer distance from the initial fire, high thermal 
inertia from the boundaries and because hot smoke and gases can be released through the openings before 
it reaches the far end. Investigations revealed that the local conditions at the far end with lower 
temperatures at the same time had a low oxygen level due to combustion of the ceiling. Low temperatures 
in combination with low oxygen levels do not support ignition, see Figure 9.36. First when either the 
temperature or oxygen level rises combustion and ignition can take place.  
The delay depends on the amount of fuel burning and the opening factor. More openings delay the ignition 
due to more hot gases being released giving reduced temperatures.  
In Figure 9.39 extracted data illustrates the drop in the oxygen volume fraction at the far end and lower 
temperatures. The rise of the temperature because of the combustion of the ceiling is initially not enough 
to support the ignition. This underlines that in a large compartment it should be considered if there are 
local areas with other premises than the in the rest of the compartment determining the local conditions 
for the fire dynamics.

indicates conditions at far end of compartment. From FDS User’s Guide Figure 15.2 [65]. 
 
 
Figure 9.37 Scenario C1.1. Left: Oxygen volume fraction before ignition of last squares in slice y = 14.5 m and 40 m < x < 45 m. The 
oxygen levels drops after ignition of timber to a level where combustion cannot take place at reduced temperatures. Right: 
Temperature in slice y = 14.5 m and 40 m < x < 45 m. A mean temperature of 375 °C is in the vicinity to support ignition of timber 
squares, see Figure 9.36. 
HRR-curve 
The HRR-curve is presented in Figure 9.38. The maximum HRR is reached at t=33 minutes before the fire 
spread between the fuel packages ceases, see Figure 9.39. This coincides with the maximum fire spread 
rate at the ceiling. At t=60 minutes all fuel packages have ceased to burn and only the ceiling is burning 
with HRR=100 kW/m².

9.16.2.3 Scenario C1.2 – Results and discussion 
Fire spread at ceiling 
In scenario C1.2 the first ignition at the ceiling took place 27 minutes after the initial fire started. The final 
square did not ignite until 80 minutes after the initial fire started and 53 minutes after the first square 
ignited, see Figure 9.40. In scenario C1.2 all timber squares except two squares in the last row got ignited. 
Same conditions as in scenario C1.1 regarding low oxygen and temperature applies here and the higher 
opening factor reinforces this effect.

9.16.2.4 Scenario C1.1 and C1.2 - Discussion 
It was unexpected that both simulations showed that introducing exposed timber at the ceiling caused the 
fire spread between the fuel packages to stop completely and that only the first three rows were involved 
in the fire. 
After the cease of the fire spread between the fuel packages only the timber ceiling squares were burning 
with HRRPUA = 100 kW/m². The HRR-curves for both scenarios are almost coinciding, see Figure 9.44. This 
is due to the early cease of the fire spread where the ventilation conditions are less important, see section 
9.13. 
Note: The continuous burning of the ceiling might not reflect a real fire scenario where the flaming 
combustion is expected to cease when all the fuel is burnt out. However, smouldering combustion can 
continue at a lower rate with reduced HRR giving a prolonged fire. Also, the burning ceiling could lead to a 
self-sustaining situation as described in section 0. As the fire spread has already ceased the influence of the 
burning ceiling on the travelling fire behaviour is not relevant in these scenarios.  
Since the fire only spread to nearby fuel packages this behaviour should not be characterized as a travelling 
fire scenario and comparison with baseline scenario regarding trailing and leading edges will not apply 
here.  
In the growth phase where the ventilation conditions are less important the ceiling ignites almost at the 
same time within a few minutes in both scenarios. When the ceiling first has ignited the fire spread rate 
along the ceiling is comparable, see Figure 9.45. This means that the opening factor does not affect the fire 
spread until the decay occurs.  
==PAGE:74==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
62 
 
 
 
Figure 9.44 HRR-curves with 10 % exposed timber at ceiling. The peaks indicate 
where the fire spread of the fuel packages ceased and the fire decayed. In 
scenario C1.2 two squares did not ignite which explains the small difference in 
the HRR-output after t=60 minutes. 
 
Figure 9.45 Comparison of fire spread rates along the ceiling. The curves are 
parallel until the decline. The higher opening factor O=0.10 m½ in C1.2 significantly 
reduces the fire spread rate in the decay period due to lower temperatures. 
In scenario C1.2 the higher opening factor increases the flow of hot gases out of the openings lowering the 
temperature in the compartment. When the fuel packages have stopped burning the hot gases are 
released through the openings and this efficient ventilation conditions and cooling causes the delay of the 
ignition of the last row at the ceiling. In scenario C1.1 the lower opening factor causes higher temperatures 
in the compartment which supports the ignition of the last row of timber squares at the ceiling. 
The gas temperature in scenario C1.2 in the smoke layer is about 300 °C, see Figure 9.46, but the surface 
temperature of the timber ceiling is not that high due to thermal properties and inertia of the boundaries. 
The timber squares reached a maximum surface temperature of 270 °C which was not enough to start the 
burner with the onset temperature 300 °C. 
In scenario C1.1 the gas temperature is about 400 °C and this is sufficient for the timber ceiling to reach a 
temperature above the onset temperature of 300 °C, see Figure 9.46. When the timber ignites the gas

9.16.2.5 Investigation of the fire spread cease 
Combustion of a timber ceiling has multiple effects on a compartment fire such as consuming more oxygen, 
producing more smoke and altering the overall fire dynamics with elevated mass flow velocities and 
temperatures. Introducing more fuel to a compartment should lead to a higher HRR and a more vigorous 
fire, given the ventilation-conditions is not a restraining factor. Therefore, this behaviour with cease of the 
fire was very counter-intuitive and needed some further research.  
Oxygen volume fraction 
Introduction of the combustible timber ceiling could have the effect that all the oxygen in the compartment 
got consumed when the timber ignited leading to an insufficient oxygen fraction for the fuel packages to 
sustain burning. This was investigated by introducing slices in the FDS-model to visualize the oxygen level, 
see section 9.9.2. 
The visualization of the slices illustrated that the compartment was divided into two layers with a low 
oxygen level of 10 – 12 % at the upper layer (2 m z < 5 m) and an oxygen volume fraction of > 20 % at the 
bottom layer (0 m < z < 2 m), see Figure 9.47. Combustion of the timber ceiling consumes oxygen and the 
oxygen level decreases but it does not impact the oxygen level at the fuel packages. Since these 
visualizations indicated enough oxygen to support the fire spread, this was ruled out as the reason for the 
cease of fire.

Gas and surface temperature 
The gas temperatures between the fuel packages in row 3 and row 4 at (x,y,z) = (6.5 m, 7.5 m, 2.0 m) is 
higher in scenario C1.1 with exposed timber than in scenario B1.1 with no timber, see Figure 9.48, up until 
cease of the fire spread at t = 40 min. This makes sense since more fuel is combusting leading to overall 
higher temperatures. So, the lack of fire spread could not initially be caused by lower gas temperatures.    
The surface temperatures of the fuel packages in the row, that did not ignite, were investigated. The fire 
spread ceased at row 4. The surface temperature of fuel package FP4.4 (face x1), see section 9.11.3, is 
illustrated in Figure 9.49 for the baseline case B1.1 with no timber present compared to scenario C1.1. The 
onset temperature for the pyrolysis is 300 °C. Temperatures below this level will not initiate the burning of 
the fuel. In the case with the exposed timber at the ceiling the maximum temperature of the surface of the 
fuel package is 282 °C just below 300 °C.

The combustion of the timber ceiling increases the temperature 
but the temperature decays as the fire spread of fuel packages 
ceases.

Floor temperature 
Another theory was that the fire dynamics in the compartment changed with more vigorous air movement 
and entrainment of cold air through the openings leading to cooling of the floor. This could explain the 
cease of the fire spread. The temperatures of the floor (z =0 m) were investigated by retrieving data using 
the fds2ascii DOS-command for the &BNDF data.  
Data for the “wall temperatures” of the floor from the submesh &MESH ID ='mesh 2.3' XB=4.5,8.5, 4.5,10.5, 
0.0,5.0, see input file in Appendix A: FDS-input file , were extracted. Only the data within the area defined 
by XB=6.125,6.875, 4.50,10.50, 0,0 between row number 3 and row number 4 were used since the data for 
the top and bottom of fuel packages and obstacles were of no interest here. The time span in interest is 
28.5 min < t < 33 min since this is in this time span the curves start to diverge. This data was compared to 
baseline scenario B1.1 and the results are listed in Table 9.6.  
From extracted data, see Table 9.6, it was clear that the floor temperatures were not significantly divergent 
and thus not explained the cease of fire.

Mesh resolution 
In section 9.13 we found based on the sensitivity analyses that a mesh resolution with δx = 0.125 m 
underpredicted the temperature with 5-10 %. Since we are in the proximity for the model (surface 
temperature of the fuel package just below 300 °C) where a finer mesh could alter the result giving slightly 
higher surface temperatures the same simulations but with a finer mesh resolution with δx = 0.100 m need 
to be carried out. These scenarios are named C2.1 and C2.2 and are discussed in sections 9.16.3.2 and 
9.16.3.3. 
9.16.2.6 Scenarios C1.1 and C1.2 - Conclusion 
Two simulations were carried out to investigate how introducing 10 % (with respect to the total area of 
compartment walls and ceiling) exposed timber at the ceiling would affect the behaviour of the travelling 
fire with no exposed timber present. The simulations were carried out for opening factors O=0.05 m½ and 
O=0.10 m½ with a mesh resolution δx = 0.125 m.  
The simulations showed that the opening factor does not influence the average fire spread rate along the 
ceiling but prolongs the ignition time of the timber squares at the far end of the compartment.  
The travelling fire behaviour in the baseline scenarios (B1.1 and B1.2) with no exposed timber present were 
significantly influenced by introduction of the exposed timber ceiling. The simulations showed 
unexpectedly that the fire spread between the fuel packages stopped after the third row. Hereafter only 
the timber ceiling was burning.  This scenario did not resemble a travelling fire but resembled a localized 
fire at the end of the compartment where the initial fire started. Therefore, results for travelling fire speed 
and propagation did not apply. 
The surface temperature of the nearest fuel package that did not ignite reached its maximum at 282 °C, just 
below the onset temperature 300 °C. Investigation of oxygen volume fraction and gas and surface 
==PAGE:78==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
66 
 
 
temperatures did not indicate any reason for the fire spread to stop. It was then concluded that a finer 
mesh was needed for these scenarios. These scenarios C2.1 and C2.2 are discussed in the next section. 
A summary of the results is presented in Table 9.7.

9.16.3 Scenarios C2.1 and C2.2 
Scenarios C2.1 and C2.2. are similar to scenarios C1.1 and C1.2 respectively. The only parameter that is 
changed is the mesh resolution that is refined from δx = 0.125 m to δx = 0.100 m, see Table 9.5, to see if this 
can prevent the fire spread cease discovered in scenarios C1.1 and C1.2.  
9.16.3.1 Scenario C2.1 - Results and discussion 
Mesh resolution 
In Figure 9.50 the surface temperature of the fuel package FP4.4 (face x1) in row 4 is illustrated. As opposed 
to the scenario C1.1 the surface temperature now exceeds the ignition limit of 300 °C and thus ignites the 
fuel packages leading to a fire spread throughout the compartment. The process is self-reinforcing as more 
fuel packages generates more heat, again igniting more fuel packages generating more heat and so 
forward. When the ceiling ignites, the process accelerates. 
The use of a finer and more sensitive mesh with δx = 0.100 m resulted in temperature output values that 
had slightly increased compared to the mesh with δx = 0.125 m. This was just enough to ensure the fire 
spread between the fuel packages. The assumption that the cell size was the determining factor in scenario 
C1.1 was then confirmed.  
 
Figure 9.50 Surface temperatures of fuel package FP4.4 (face x1) in the 
middle of row 4, see Figure 9.55. The temperature now exceeds 300 °C in 
scenario C2.1 causing the burner to start as opposed to scenario C1.1 with 
the mesh of δx = 0.125 m.

HRR-curve 
The HRR-curve is presented in Figure 9.51. The steep inclination of the curve starts when all the timber at 
the ceiling have been ignited and the curve peaks when all the rest of the fuel packages have been ignited.  
Compared to the baseline scenario B2.1 with no timber present, there is a much more rapid increase of 
HRR as expected and the maximum HRR is increased by nearly 30 % due to the increased amount of fire 
load.  
The duration of the fire with exposed timber is 40 minutes shorter than the baseline scenario as more fuel 
packages are ignited simultaneously and not in succession. This could, despite the higher HRR, benefit the 
structures if it does not yield as high temperatures as the long fire duration with no timber. This is further 
discussed later in this section.  
Note: The baseline scenario B2.1 with δx = 0.100 m is used as benchmark in this section to make the 
comparison of the results independent of the mesh resolution.  
 
Figure 9.51 HRR-curve for scenario C2.1 with δx=0.100 m compared to baseline scenario B2.1 with 
δx=0.100 m. In the steady state (31 min < t < 46 min) the difference in the HRR-curve corresponds to 
the HRR of the burning ceiling with HRRPUA=100 kW/m². This means that before flashover the 
travelling fire is not significantly affected by the burning ceiling.  
Fire spread at ceiling 
In this scenario all the timber squares got ignited. The first ignition at the ceiling took place 23 minutes 
after the initial fire started, see Figure 9.52. The fire spread rate decays at the far end of the compartment 
(34 m < x) due to lower temperatures an oxygen conditions as described in scenario C1.1, see section 
9.16.2.2. 
The fire spread behaviour is similar to scenario C1.1, see Figure 9.53. The ignition of all the fuel packages 
does not affect the fire spread rate as the whole ceiling has already been ignited at this point and the fire 
spread between the fuel packages are alike before t = 40 min. Refining the mesh resolution to δx = 0.100 
increased the average fire spread rate along the ceiling with 13 % compared to scenario C1.1 with mesh 
resolution δx = 0.125 m.

Travelling fire behaviour 
The progression of the fire is illustrated in Figure 9.54. The fire has a steady progress in the beginning with a 
travel speed of 0.2 m/min, which is similar to the baseline scenario, see Figure 9.25. As more and more of 
the timber ceiling gets ignited, the travel speed quickly accelerates. Flashover occurs at t=55 min involving 
all the fuel packages in the fire, see Figure 9.55. This behaviour significantly diverges from the scenario with 
no timber present and should only under the steady state t < 46 min be characterized as a travelling fire.

Figure 9.54 Flame front progression for scenario C2.1. Yellow square shows location of the initial fire. Red dotted lines indicate

Boundary temperatures 
Using the fds2ascii DOS-command, the mean boundary temperatures for both end walls were extracted at 
the end of the fire duration. In the cooling and decay phase the heat keeps transferring through the 
structures leading to the end of the fire as the most severe scenario, see Figure 9.57.  
Note: The heat keeps transferring through the structures after the fuel is burnt out but this data is not 
available. 
Despite the high rise in HRR for the scenario with exposed timber at the ceiling and the increased fire load, 
the surface temperatures of the end wall (x = 45 m) are lower compared to the baseline scenario with no 
exposed timber. For the end wall (x= 0 m) closest to the initial fire the difference is less significant. 
This difference can be explained by the time factor. The end wall at x = 45 m will from the fire starts 
experience heat as the hot smoke travels along the ceiling. As the fire travels towards the end wall this 
further impacts the wall leading to increased temperatures. The end wall (x = 0 m) closest to the initial fire 
experiences a high impact of heat at the beginning but is exposed to a shorter duration of heat as the fire 
travels away from the wall and the hot smoke is released through the openings.

while the “cold end” at x= 0 m experiences heat impact for at shorter duration of time.

The gas temperatures in the middle of the compartment at (y,z) = (7.5 m, 3.0 m) are illustrated in Figure 
9.58 at different x-positions along the compartment. The temperatures are inhomogeneous even after 
flashover. For t= 60 min the maximum temperature is nearly 60 % higher than the minimum temperature. 
After burnout of fuel packages at t = 80 min, the temperature in the compartment decreases to a steady 
state as only the ceiling is burning with a constant HRR.

9.16.3.2 Scenario C2.1 – Conclusion 
I this section the scenario with opening factor O= 0.05 m½ and 10 % exposed timber at the ceiling with a  
HRR = 100 kW/m² was investigated. The scenario is similar to scenario C1.1 except the mesh resolution was 
refined to investigate if too coarse a mesh was the reason for the cease of the fire spread in scenario C1.1, 
see section 9.16.2.  
Altering the mesh from δx = 0.125 m to δx = 0.100 m resulted in output surface temperatures for the fuel 
packages just exceeding the onset temperature of 300 °C. This led to the effect that the fire spread did not 
cease, as was the case for scenario C1.1, and all fuel packages got ignited. The model with δx=0.125 m was 
just at the proximity for the onset temperature and burning to take place as the temperatures were slightly

underpredicted. This emphasizes that a sensitivity analysis is crucial to make the right assumptions and 
conclusions.  
The fire spread along the ceiling had the same behaviour as scenario C1.1 but the average velocity 
increased with 13 % due to the finer mesh resolution yielding slightly higher temperatures.   
Introducing timber at the ceiling altered the behaviour of the travelling fire after t = 40 minutes. Up until 
this point the fire propagation was similar as the baseline scenario, but when all the ceiling had ignited the 
travelling fire turned into a flashover scenario with a high HRR.  
The gas temperatures in the center of the compartment were inhomogeneous even after flashover and 
levelled at a lower steady state after burnout of all fuel packages.  
An important observation was that the flashover scenario is not necessarily the worst case regarding the 
structures. A travelling fire with a long fire duration could be the worst-case scenario due to longer heat 
impact of structures, even if the fire load is lower than a scenario with exposed timber present.

9.16.3.3 Scenario C2.2 – Results and discussion 
Mesh resolution 
The purpose of this scenario was to investigate whether a finer mesh with δx = 0.100 m would slightly 
increase the surface temperatures to support a fire spread involving all the fuel packages. 
However, in this scenario with an opening factor O=0.10 m½ the fire spread was not supported and the fire 
stopped at row 5. Compared to scenario C1.2, see Figure 9.43, the finer mesh with slightly elevated 
temperatures resulted in 5 more fuel packages to get involved before the fire spread ceased.  
==PAGE:85==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
73 
 
 
Fire spread at ceiling 
In scenario C2.2 all the timber squares got ignited as the fine mesh resolution yielded elevated 
temperatures. The first ignition at the ceiling took place 25 minutes after the initial fire. The behaviour of 
the fire spread rates along the ceiling is similar to scenario C1.2, see Figure 9.61 except the ignition takes 
places 2 minutes earlier due to the slightly elevated temperatures with the finer mesh. As in the previous 
discussed scenarios there is a significant delay before the final squares ignites at t=89 min due to local 
conditions with low temperature and oxygen and time is needed to build up sufficient heat and energy. 
Two squares at the last row ignites at t=48-49 min. This consumes oxygen and delays the ignition of the last 
square.

9.16.3.4 Scenario C2.2 – Conclusion 
The scenario with an opening factor O= 0.10 m½ and 10 % exposed timber at the ceiling with a  
HRR = 100 kW/m² was investigated. The scenario is similar to scenario C1.2 except the mesh resolution was 
refined from δx = 0.125 m to δx = 0.100 m to investigate if too coarse a mesh was the reason for the cease of 
the fire spread in scenario C1.2.  
In this case refining the mesh did not support the fire spread. The finer mesh with slightly elevated 
temperatures caused 5 more fuel packages to ignite but the fire spread ceased at row 5. The average fire 
spread rate along the ceiling was not significantly altered with the finer mesh, but the ceiling ignited 2 
minutes earlier due to the slightly elevated temperatures in the finer mesh. 
Despite investigations of the oxygen fraction, temperatures and mass flow through the openings, see 
Appendix D: Investigation of fire spread cease in Scenario C2.2, it was not possible to give a final 
explanation of the fire cease. Almost all acknowledged models for fire dynamics and heat energy balances 
rely on the assumption that the fire source is on the floor with a vertical buoyant gas stream. Introducing a 
fire source at the ceiling with horizontal and downwards burning will challenge most of the theory as the 
fire dynamics will be significantly altered. There is to this authors knowledge no current literature that fully 
describes this case, even though some recent experiments have been carried out [4]. 
It is not verified whether the introduction of the timber ceiling causes disturbances to a degree that the fire 
ceases, or if is it due to limitations in the FDS-modelling, or a combination of both. It is strongly 
recommended that this is investigated further. However, this is not possible within the time frame of this 
thesis and is recommended as a future research topic. It is however evident that a HRR of 100 kW/m² 
corresponding to a charring rate of β=0.7 mm/min for the timber ceiling is just at the limit for the fire 
spread between the fuel packages to take place. The disturbances this burning ceiling might have on the 
overall fire dynamics seems to outpower the potential of the extra energy release of the burning timber. 
This is why any increase of either the HRR or the amount of exposed timber presumably should lead to a 
fire spread. This is analyzed in section 9.16.5 and 9.16.6.

9.16.5 Scenarios C3.1 and C3.2 
9.16.5.1 Scenario C3.1 
This scenario is similar to scenario C1.1 except in this scenario the area of the exposed timber at the ceiling 
is increased from 10 % to 20 % with respect to the walls and ceiling area, see Table 9.5. The opening factor 
is O=0.05 m½ and the timber ceiling has a HRR = 100 kW/m².  
The scenario is relevant to see the impact on the fire by doubling the area of the exposed timber. Choosing 
20 % is based on the limit in the Danish regulations for exposed timber without further documentation 
(pre-accepted solution, see section 5). It is also relevant to see if a doubling of the amount of exposed 
timber at the ceiling will support a fire spread between the fuel packages as this was not the case with 10 % 
exposed timber in scenario C2.2. 
The exposed timber is distributed on the ceiling in 22 squares of 3 m x 3 m and 11 squares of 2 m x 2 m, a 
total area of 242 m², see Figure 9.63. Each square of timber is located inside one submesh to ensure the 
square does not overlap meshes as this could disturb the data transfer during the simulation.  
Each square is modelled with the command &VENT. When just one point in the square reaches 300 °C, the 
whole surface of the square starts to burn. This is a valid assumption given the temperature at the ceiling 
within the limited timber areas is homogenous.  
9.16.5.2 Fire load 
The total amount of fire load of the timber is  
𝑄𝑡𝑖𝑚𝑏𝑒𝑟= 𝐻𝑅𝑅𝑃𝑈𝐴∗𝐴∗𝑡= 100 𝑘𝑊
𝑚2 ∗(22 ∗9 + 11 ∗4)𝑚2 ∗3,600 𝑠= 87 𝐺𝐽 
This load is added to the fire load of the fuel packages giving a total fire load of  
𝑄𝑡𝑜𝑡𝑎𝑙= 154 ∗𝑄𝐹𝑃+ 𝑄𝑡𝑖𝑚𝑏𝑒𝑟= 154 ∗1.315 𝐺𝐽+ 87 𝐺𝐽= 290 𝐺𝐽 
9.16.5.3 Results and discussion 
Fire spread at the ceiling 
The first ignition of the ceiling took place 24 minutes after the initial fire started. Hereafter the fire spread 
rate is similar to scenario C2.1 with 10 % exposed timber, but then the fire spread rate increases. This is 
expected as the higher HRR due to the increased amount of timber accelerates the process of both the fire 
==PAGE:88==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
76 
 
 
spread among the fuel packages and the fire spread along the ceiling. The burning ceiling supports the fire 
spread of the fuel packages and before the whole ceiling has ignited, the heat release from the burning fuel 
packages supports the fire spread along the ceiling.  
 
 
Figure 9.63 Scenario C3.1. 33 timber squares with a total area of 242 m² located at the ceiling. 
Squares in the first and third horizontal row have a surface area of 3 m x 3 m and squares in the 
middle row has a surface area of 2 m x 2 m. The area of the exposed timber corresponds to 20 % 
with respect to the total surface area of walls and ceiling of the compartment. 
At x = 34 m the fire spread rate decays, see Figure 9.64. This is the same behaviour as seen in the previous 
discussed scenarios. The fire spread at the ceiling travels faster than the travelling fire, see Figure 9.66 and 
reaches the “cold end” of the compartment before the heat release from the fuel packages have had a 
significant impact on this far end, see Figure 9.65. This slows down the fire spread along the ceiling as more 
energy is needed to heat the timber squares at the last row at the far end.  
As revealed in scenario C1.1 in section 9.16.2.2 the far end with lower temperatures and reduced oxygen 
decays the fire spread rate. In this scenario the extra heat release from the ceiling increases the 
temperature at a faster rate and the decay is less pronounced.  
Note: The scenario is compared to scenario C2.1 with δx =0.100 m because scenario C1.1 with δx =0.125 m 
did not support fire spread between fuel packages. Scenario C2.1 yields slightly elevated values which 
should be taken into account.

HRR-curve 
Increasing the exposed timber area from 10 % to 20 % produces sufficient heat to involve all the fuel 
packages in the fire with a mesh resolution δx = 0.125 m. A finer mesh is not necessary as we are well above 
the onset temperature of the fuel packages. The HRR-curve is illustrated in Figure 9.67. After t = 64 min 
only the ceiling is burning with HRRPUA = 100 kW/m².  
The HRR-curve for scenario C3.1 is compared with scenario C2.1, where all the fuel packages got ignited.  
Note: Comparison with scenario C2.1 is used instead of scenario C1.1 which not immediately apply as the 
fire spread ceased in this scenario.  
==PAGE:90==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
78 
 
 
 
Figure 9.67 HRR-curves for scenario C3.1 with opening factor 0.05 m½ and 20 % exposed timber 
compared to scenario C2.1 with 10 % exposed timber and opening factor O=0.05 m½. Peak HRR is in 
both scenarios 91 MW indicating this is the limit for the maximum heat release rate in this 
compartment (ventilation-controlled fire).  
Doubling the area of the exposed timber at the ceiling causes a flashover scenario to take place 13 minutes 
earlier. The maximum HRR and duration of the fire is the same.  
Just after flashover the HRR drops. This is due to excess of pyrolysis gasses and not sufficient oxygen to 
combust inside which forces the combustion to take place outside. When numerous fuel packages have 
burnt out the HRR increases, now having more available oxygen in the compartment. When all fuel is burnt 
out the HRR drops to the level where only the ceiling is burning at a steady rate. 
As was the case for scenario C2.1 the maximum HRR is 91 MW. Since this is the limit regardless of the area 
of the ceiling burning, this indicates that this is the limit for the HRR of the fire in this compartment with 
opening factor O=0.05 m½. 
At t=45 min the whole ceiling is burning with HRR=Aceiling*HRRceiling=242*100 kW = 24 MW 
A t = 45 t min 133 fuel packages (19 rows of 7 packages) are burning simultaneously with a potential HRR of 
133*1,500 kW = 200 MW. This confirms that 91 MW is the HRR-limit and where the fire gets ventilation-
controlled.  
From section 6.3 we found that the limit for the ventilation-controlled fire (for small compartments) was 
𝐻𝑅𝑅𝑉𝐶=  0.09 ∗𝐴𝑜√𝐻𝑜∗∆𝐻𝑐,𝑤𝑜𝑜𝑑 
In this case this would give  
𝐻𝑅𝑅𝑉𝐶=  0.09 ∗48√4 ∗17,500 𝑘𝑊
𝑘𝑔= 151 𝑀𝑊> 91 𝑀𝑊 
 
This formula is as expected not applicable for large compartments as other factor influences the maximum 
heat release.

The duration of the fire is determined by the HRR-curve of the ceiling. Each square of the timber ceiling is 
programmed to have a fire duration of 60 min. As the last square in all scenarios ignites almost at the same 
time, the duration of the fire becomes the same within a few minutes.  
Travelling fire behaviour 
The behaviour of the travelling fire is significantly affected by the burning timber ceiling. The high HRR and 
increased temperatures support a rapid fire spread among the fuel packages, see Figure 9.68.  
A travelling fire is not the case, but the fire is more characterized as a localised fire that turns into a 
flashover scenario. The analysis of trailing and leading edge does not apply in this scenario.

9.16.5.4 Scenario C3.2 
This scenario is similar to scenario C3.1 in section 9.16.5 except the opening factor is increased from 
0.05 m½ to 0.10 m½. The timber ceiling has a HRR = 100 kW/m².  See Table 9.5 for data. 
9.16.5.5 Results and discussion 
In this simulation the fire spread between the fuel packages completely ceased before row 4 at t= 58 min 
but resumed at t=65 m, see HHR-curve in Figure 9.73.  
At t = 65 min the heat from the burning ceiling and hot smoke layer ignited the rest of the fuel packages. 
This led to flashover at t=75 minutes. The fuel packages nearest the boundary and between the opening got 
ignited first representing the area in the compartment with the highest temperatures, see Figure 9.69. 
 
Figure 9.69 Scenario C3.2 at t=67 min. The hot smoke layer causes ignition of the remaining 
fuel packages and the fire spread resumes. The fuel packages between the openings are the 
first to ignite (highlighted red squares) where the temperature is highest. 
==PAGE:92==

Fire spread at ceiling 
The first ignition at the ceiling took place 27 minutes after the initial fire started, see Figure 9.70. The delay 
of the ignition with 3 minutes compared to scenario C3.1 is expected as the higher opening factor 
decreases the temperatures.  
The fire spread pattern is similar to scenario C3.1 but has a slower spread rate due to higher opening factor 
and lower temperatures. Compared to scenario C2.2 with 10 % timber at ceiling the average spread rate is 
twice as high, see Figure 9.72.

Travelling fire behaviour 
The behaviour of the travelling fire is significantly affected by the burning timber ceiling. The travelling fire 
ceases at t=20 and then only spreads sidewards without moving forward.  
A travelling fire is not the case, but the fire is more characterized as a localised fire that turns into a 
flashover scenario. The analysis of trailing and leading edge does not apply in this scenario.  
 
Figure 9.74 Scenario C3.2. Flame front (trailing edge) positions at different times. Yellow square shows location of the initial fire. 
Red dotted lines indicate openings. The fire spread stops at row 3 until t = 60 minutes. Then it resumes and spreads rapidly due to 
the high overall temperatures.  
9.16.5.6 Scenario C3.1 and C3.2 – Conclusion 
Two scenarios with 20 % exposed timber at the ceiling and opening factor O=0.05 m½ and O=0.10 m½ were 
carried out to investigate the influence of the amount of the exposed timber on the travelling fire 
behaviour.  
In both scenarios the heat impact from the burning ceiling highly affected the behaviour of the travelling 
fire and the led to flashover scenarios. The higher opening factor delayed the flashover by 33 minutes from 
42 to 75 minutes.  
The HRR-curves indicated proportionality between the opening factor and the maximum HRR for the 
ventilation-controlled fire as suggested by Kawagoe for small compartments. More data is however 
necessary to make any final conclusions and further research on this topic is highly recommended.  
The opening factor and amount of exposed timber only affected the ignition time of the ceiling with a few 
minutes because the ventilation and boundary conditions are less influential at the growth phase.  
A doubling of the timber amount from 10 % to 20 % led to a significant (76 - 96 %) increase of the average 
fire spread rate at the ceiling, and the flashover for opening factor O=0.05 m½ took place 13 minutes earlier 
which corresponds to a 25 % time reduction. 
The average fire spread velocity at the ceiling was not significantly affected by the opening factor. However, 
the maximum fire spread velocity is twice as high for the opening factor 0.05 m½ compared to opening 
factor 0.10 m½.  
The fire spread rate decreased at the far end of the compartment where there are lower temperatures and 
lower oxygen level which hinders and delays the ignition.  
==PAGE:95==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
83

9.16.6 Scenarios C4.1 and C4.2 
In scenario C2.1 and C2.2 it was concluded that HRR = 100 kW/m² for the timber ceiling was just at the limit 
to support fire spread among the fuel packages. To investigate the influence of the HRR on the travelling 
fire behaviour the HRR for the timber is increased to 150 kW/m² in scenario C4.1 and C4.2. This 
corresponds to a charring rate β = 1.0 mm/min, see section 9.12. The total burning time of the timber 
squares is reduced to 40 minutes to give the same char depth of ~ 40 mm corresponding to a charring rate 
β = 0.7 mm/min and 60 minutes burning time. 
All other parameters are as in scenario C1.1 and C1.2 with 10 % exposed timber, see Table 9.5. 
Note: The results are compared to scenario C2.1 with mesh resolution δ x= 0.100 m as fire spread ceased in 
scenario C1.1. The fine mesh resolution with δ x= 0.100 m yields slightly higher values which should be kept 
in mind when comparing the results.  
9.16.6.1 Scenario C4.1 – Results and discussion  
Fire spread at ceiling 
The ignition of the ceiling took place 25 minutes after the initial fire. The fire progressed along the ceiling 
reaching the middle of the far end at 39 minutes, see Figure 9.75. The squares at the far end ignited within 
minutes of each other. 
Altering the HRR from 100 kW/m² to 150 kW/m² should lead to an increased fire spread velocity and this 
assumption is supported by the results showing a velocity increase of 50 %, see Figure 9.76.

Figure 9.76 Scenario C4.1. Fire spread rate at ceiling compared to scenario

HRR-curve 
The HRR-curve is presented in Figure 9.77. The HRR-curve shows same pattern as scenario C2.1 with 
HRR=100 kW/m² but increasing the HRR to 150 kW/m² accelerates the process with flashover taking place 9 
minutes earlier. At t=80 minutes all combustibles have burnt out. The burning time of the timber was 
reduced to 40 minutes giving a shorter overall fire duration than in scenario C2.1 with burning time 60 
minutes.  
The HRR drops at t = 60 minutes due to combustion and reduction of oxygen forcing the combustion to 
take place outside and rises to 91 MW when the oxygen level increases due to air entrainment, see Figure 
9.78. 
The maximum HRR is 91 MW being the limit for the ventilation-controlled fire as seen in the previous 
scenarios.

Travelling fire behaviour 
The behaviour of the travelling fire is also in this scenario highly affected by the burning ceiling. The

9.16.6.2 Scenario C4.2 – Results and discussion  
This scenario is similar to scenario C4.1 except the opening factor is doubled from 0.05 m½ to 0.10 m½.  
In this scenario the fire spread between the fuel packages ceased even with an increased HRR of the timber 
ceiling of 150 kW/m². This means that the opening factor has a high influence on the fire behaviour and 
outpowers the potential of the heat release of the timber ceiling.  It should be noted that an increase of the 
HRR from 100 kW/m² to 150 kW/m² was expected to support the fire spread.  
Fire spread at ceiling 
The ignition of the ceiling took place 27 minutes after the initial fire. The fire progressed along the ceiling 
reaching the middle of the far end at 45 minutes, see Figure 9.80. The squares at the far end ignited within 
minutes of each other. 
Altering the HRR from 100 kW/m² to 150 kW/m² should lead to an increased fire spread velocity. The high 
opening factor and cooling of the compartment limits this effect and the average fire spread velocity only 
increases with 5 %.  
The scenario showed the same behaviour as the previous investigated scenarios where there is a rapid fire 
spread until position x = 34 m, where the spread rate declines and delays the ignition of the squares at the 
far end. The reason behind this has been thoroughly investigated and discussed in section 9.16.2.2. Please 
refer to this section.   
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:99==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
87 
 
 
 
 
Figure 9.80 Scenario C4.2. Ignition times for timber squares at ceiling with HRR= 150 kW/m².

is shorter than scenario C1.2. The timber squares in scenario C4.2 has a burning time of 40 minutes to give 
the same char depth of ~ 40 mm as with the lower charring rate.

9.16.6.3 Scenarios C4.1 and C4.2 – Conclusion  
Two scenarios with 10 % exposed timber at the ceiling and opening factor O=0.05 m½ and O=0.10 m½ were 
carried out. The heat release rate of the timber was increased to HRR = 150 kW/m² to see the effect on the 
fire behaviour and fire spread.  
The fire spread rate along the ceiling was increased with 50 % in the case with O=0.05 m2 compared to 
scenario C2.1 with HRR=100 kW/m². In the case with the high opening factor O=0.10 m½ the increase was 
only 5 %. The opening factor has a high influence on the fire behaviour. This seems to outpower the effect 
the increased heat release might have on the fire spread rate.   
The behaviour of the travelling fire was significantly changed. The fire resembled a localized fire that turned 
into flashover in scenario C4.1. Investigating trailing and leading edges did therefore not apply. 
In scenario C4.2 with opening factor 0.10 m½ the fire spread between the fuel packages ceased at row 3. 
The altered fire dynamics with a combusting ceiling, even with a high HRR of 150 kW/m², seems to 
overpower the fire spread of the fuel packages as was the case in scenario C1.2 with HRR=100 kW/m².  
The fire spread cease between the fuel packages for scenario C1.2 was investigated and discussed in 
section 9.16.2.4. Please refer to this section for further information. Further investigation regarding this 
phenomenon is highly recommended and encouraged but is unfortunately out of the time limits for this 
thesis.

9.16.7 Gas temperatures 
In Figure 9.83 the time-temperature curves in the middle of the compartment (x,y,z)=(22.5 m, 7.5 m, 3.0 m)

timber accelerates the process of the fire propagation with approximately 10 minutes.

9.16.8 Exposed timber ceiling – Conclusion 
A total of 8 scenarios were carried out to investigate the behaviour of the travelling fire in a large 
compartment with a partially exposed timber ceiling. The opening factors were 0.05 m½ and 0.10 m½. The 
amount of exposed timber was 10 % and 20 % and the heat release rate of the timber was 100 kW/m² and  
150 kW/m². The mesh resolution was δx = 0.125 m except for two scenarios where it was refined to  
δx = 0.100 m. The main findings are summarized below.  
The mesh resolution was found to be an important factor when results were in the proximity where just a 
small change could alter the fire behaviour. The mesh resolution should therefore always be assessed 
thoroughly in any FDS-simulations even if it complies with the sensitivity analysis.  
The introduction of exposed timber at the ceiling significantly changed the behaviour of the travelling fire 
compared to the baseline scenarios with no exposed timber present. A travelling fire was only present at 
the very beginning before it turned into a flashover scenario. An important finding was that in a scenario 
with exposed timber at the ceiling a flashover occurred at an early stage but was not necessarily the worst-
case scenario regarding the structures. This is due to the shorter fire duration. The travelling fire with no 
exposed timber has a long fire duration that impacts the structures for a longer time leading to elevated 
temperatures.  
Doubling the amount of exposed timber from 10 % to 20 % increased the fire spread rate at the ceiling with 
50 % and accelerated the fire propagation and flashover occurrence with approximately 10 minutes.  
The opening factor had little influence on the ignition time of the ceiling, but the high opening factor 
reduced the fire spread rate at the ceiling.  
The large compartment showed inhomogeneous conditions and local conditions that might occur in a fire 
scenario in a large compartment should always be considered. The local conditions at the far end of the 
compartment (opposite the initial fire) highly affected the fire spread along the ceiling. In all scenarios the 
fire spread rate decayed at the far end and in some cases ignition of the ceiling did not appear. Locally low 
temperatures and low oxygen volume fraction in combination led to conditions that did not support 
ignition.  
The HRR-curves indicated proportionality between the opening factor and the maximum HRR for the 
ventilation-controlled fire as suggested by Kawagoe for small compartments. More data is however 
necessary to make any final conclusions and further research on this topic is highly recommended.  
The gas temperatures in the compartment were inhomogeneous even after flashover with high near field 
temperatures at the travelling fire and lower temperatures at the far field. The assumption of uniform 
temperatures in a fire compartment for a fully developed fire is highly questioned.  
In the scenarios with opening factor 0.10 m½ a complete cease of the fire spread between the fuel packages 
unexpectedly took place except in the scenario with 20 % exposed timber. Despite thorough investigations 
it was not possible to make any final conclusions regarding the fire spread cease and further research is 
highly recommended.  Regardless, this emphasizes that exposed timber in a fire design context can make 
the fire dynamics unpredictable and puts great demands on the fire safety engineer.

9.17 
Exposed timber walls  
In these scenarios parts of the concrete walls are substituted with exposed timber. The surface area of the 
exposed timber is stated with respect to the total surface area of the walls and ceilings of the compartment 
(1,250 m²). In the Danish regulations [41], see section 5, the area of exposed timber allowed is with respect 
to this area. To make it comparable, the same reference area is used here. The rest of the walls and the 
ceiling is concrete. See Table 9.5 for data. 
The timber walls are located at the farthest end from the initial fire. The location is chosen as an exposed 
timber surface too close to the initial fire would presumably lead to almost instantly ignition which would 
hinder investigation of the travelling fire.  
9.17.1 FDS-modelling 
The end wall and parts of the adjacent side walls are modelled as timber surfaces supplied with FDS-
command &VENT covering the surface. Each surface of the timber walls is divided into 5 squares each with 
height of 1 m, see Figure 9.86. This is to account for any temperature differences along the wall height. It 
would be inaccurate to assume that the whole wall has a uniform temperature along the height. 
When just one point in the square reaches 300 °C, the whole surface of the square starts to burn. This is a 
valid assumption as the temperature is expected to be approximately homogeneous at the same height. 
This assumption is proved by extracting data using the fds2ascii-command, see Figure 9.84 and Figure 9.85.  
 
 
==PAGE:104==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
92 
 
 
Surface temperature of exposed timber walls 
 
 
Figure 9.84 Scenario W1.1. Left: Surface temperature along end wall before ignition at t=98 min in upper square 4 m z < 5 m. The 
temperature is homogeneous along the y-direction as assumed. Right: Surface temperature along side wall before ignition at t=98 
min in upper square 4 m z < 5 m. The temperature is homogeneous along the x-direction as assumed. Temperatures are lowest 
where the walls intersect. Note: Data extracted by using the fds2ascii-command.  
 
Figure 9.85. Scenario W1.1. Visualization of boundary temperatures at t=80 min. At the transition 
between the exposed timber side walls and concrete side wall the difference in thermal inertia is 
evident. The high thermal inertia of the concrete gives lower surface temperatures. Visualization 
shows homogeneous surface temperatures at exposed timber wall within each square as assumed.  
Note: The visualization only gives a qualitative expression of the temperatures. For exact data the 
fds2scii-method must be applied.  
The timber squares on the walls are not placed inside individual submeshes as was the case for the timber 
ceiling squares. In the scenarios with exposed timber ceiling the flame spread was horizontally along the 
ceiling crossing several submeshes. In this case the flame spread is primarily vertical as the heat builds from 
the hot smoke layer. Each submesh has the full height of the compartment of 5 m and the vertical flame 
spread does not cross over submeshes.  
The burner of each vent is modelled as described in section 9.12 with HRRPUA = 100 kW/m². This 
corresponds to a charring rate β=0,7 mm/min. The total duration of the timber squares burning is 60 
minutes corresponding to a ~ 40 mm depth of char layer. 
Note: The timber walls in this compartment imitate structures with sufficient cross section to support a 
charring depth of 40 mm. In other compartments with combustible linings of a few centimeters depth the 
burning time of the timber should be correspondingly shorter.

9.17.2.2 Scenario W1.1 – Results and discussion 
Travelling fire behaviour 
Before ignition of the timber the travelling fire behaves as in baseline scenario B1.1, see section 9.15.1, and 
is not affected by the timber walls at the far end, see Figure 9.87.  
 
 
Figure 9.87 Upper: Flame front progression for scenario W1.1. Walls ignite a t=101 min. Lower: Flame front progression for 
travelling fire in baseline scenario B1.1. Red dotted lines indicate openings.  
The walls ignite at t= 101 min. At this point the travelling fire has not reached the far end, see Figure 9.87. 
This indicates the hot smoke layer is the triggering factor for ignition and not direct heat and flame 
impingement from the travelling fire, see Figure 9.88.  
==PAGE:107==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
95 
 
 
 
Figure 9.88 Scenario W1.1. Gas temperature in the middle of the compartment (y=7.5) at t=100 min 
at different x-locations. The near field at the travelling fire has elevated temperatures and the far 
field at the walls has not been directly affected by the travelling fire. Each end of the compartment 
has uniform temperatures along the height.  
The upper part of the walls (1 m< z < 5 m) ignites almost simultaneously within 10 seconds having almost 
the same temperature, see Figure 9.88. The lower parts (0 m < z < 1 m) with the lowest temperatures 
ignites 4 minutes later.  
The ignition of the walls triggers the ignition of the nearest fuel packages and the fire travels backwards 
before all fuel packages ignite with few minutes, see Figure 9.89, and flashover occurs.  
 
Figure 9.89 Scenario W1.1. Just after ignition of walls the fuel packages in the proximity ignites. 
Flashover takes places few minutes later.  
HRR-curve 
The HRR-curve is illustrated in Figure 9.90. Before ignition the curve is coinciding with the baseline scenario 
B1.1 with no exposed timber. Flashover takes places minutes after ignition of walls.

9.17.2.3 Scenario W1.2 – Results and discussion

Before ignition of the timber walls the travelling fire behaves as in baseline scenario B1.2, see section 
9.15.1, and is not affected by the timber at the far end. When the timber ignites flashover happens within 
minutes, see Figure 9.91.
