Figure 9.93 Scenario W1.2 The ignition of the walls at t=134 min occurs when the travelling fire approaches.  
 
HRR-curve 
The HRR-curve is illustrated in Figure 9.94. The ignition of the walls takes places 33 minutes later than in 
scenario W1.1 with opening factor 0.05 m½ due to lower temperatures as more hot gases released through 
the openings. 
The HRR-curve coincides with the HRR-curve for the baseline scenario B1.2 until ignition of the walls. The 
exposed timber at the far end with lower thermal inertia has no effect on the travelling fire before ignition 
due to the lesser proximity to the fire. This corresponds with the literature see e.g., Buchanan/Östman [13]. 
The upper part of the walls (1 m< z < 5 m) ignites almost simultaneously within 10 seconds. This 
corresponds with Figure 9.92 (right) with uniform temperatures at the far end. The lower parts 
(0 m < z < 1 m) with the lowest temperature ignites 3 minutes later. After burnout of fuel (t=160 min) the 
HRR-curve represents the walls are still burning with HRRPUA=100 kW/m².  
The HRR peaks at 71 MW at t=117 minutes.  At this point there is 46 fuel packages left, see Figure 9.95, 
giving a fuel load of 46*1,500 kW = 70 MW. The walls contribute with 12.5 MW. Fuel packages near the 
wall cannot combust due to low oxygen, see Figure 9.96, giving a HRR of (46-7)*1,500 = 58.5 + 12.5 = 71 
MW which corresponds to the HRR-output. 
The maximum HRR for W1.1 is higher than scenario W1.2 because flashover occurs at an earlier stage when 
there is more fuel left to contribute to the heat release rate.

giving lower temperatures delays the flashover by 33 minutes.

9.17.3 Scenario W2.1 and W2.2 
To investigate the effect of the amount of exposed timber the area is increased to 20 % with respect to the 
total area of the walls and ceilings. The opening factors are 0.05 m½ and 0.10 m½. The end wall is fully 
replaced with exposed timber and part of the adjacent side walls are replaced by exposed timber, see 
Figure 9.97. The total surface area of the exposed timber is 250 m² in scenario W2.1 and 252 m² in scenario 
W2.2. The FDS-modelling is described in section 9.17.1.  
Note: Due to the different locations of the openings in scenario W2.1 and W2.2 the location of the exposed 
timber on the side walls is slightly different in the scenarios giving a difference of the surface areas of 2 m² 
(less than 1 %) which does not affect the results.

𝑄𝑡𝑜𝑡𝑎𝑙= 154 ∗𝑄𝐹𝑃+ 𝑄𝑡𝑖𝑚𝑏𝑒𝑟= 154 ∗1.315 𝐺𝐽+ (27 + 64) 𝐺𝐽= 294 𝐺𝐽 
 
9.17.3.2 Scenario W2.1 – Results and discussion 
Travelling fire behaviour 
Before ignition of the timber walls at t=70 min the travelling fire behaves as in baseline scenario B1.1, see 
section 9.15.1, and is not affected by the timber walls at the far end, see Figure 9.98. 
 
 
Figure 9.98 Upper: Flame front progression for scenario W2.1. Walls ignite a t=70 min. Lower: Flame front progression for 
travelling fire in baseline scenario B1.1. Red dotted lines indicate openings.  
The walls ignite almost simultaneously indicating uniform temperatures. The ignition of the lower parts of 
the walls (0 m < z < 1 m) are a few minutes delayed and ignites after being heated by burning nearby fuel 
packages and the upper wall burning.  
==PAGE:114==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
102 
 
 
The ignition of the walls before direct impact from the travelling fire indicates the hot smoke layer is the 
trigger for ignition as described in scenario W1.1, see section 9.17.2.2 and Figure 9.99. The ignition of the 
walls triggers the ignition of the nearest fuel packages and the fire travels backwards, see Figure 9.100, 
before all fuel packages ignite with few minutes, and flashover occurs.  
 
Figure 9.99 Scenario W2.1 The walls ignite at t= 70 min (yellow surface) before the travelling fire has

nearby fuel packages and the fire travels backwards. Flashover takes place within a few minutes 
after the ignition of the walls.

scenario B1.1 until the walls ignites. The maximum HRR is also in this case 91 MW confirming this as the 
limit for the ventilation-controlled fire as the potential fire load for the fuel-controlled fire at this point 
would exceed 91 MW.

9.17.5 Exposed timber walls - Conclusion 
Four scenarios were carried out to investigate the influence of exposed timber walls on the travelling fire 
behaviour. The models had opening factors 0.05 m½ and 0.10 m½ and areas of the exposed timber of 10 % 
and 20 % with respect to the total area of the walls and ceiling in the compartment. The timber walls were 
located at the end of the compartment farthest from the initial fire.  
The behaviour of the travelling fire was not affected by the timber walls until ignition of the walls occurred. 
All walls ignited almost simultaneously, and flashover occurred within minutes. The reduced thermal inertia 
of the timber walls did not affect the travelling fire as the walls were not in the proximity of the fire. 
Once the timber walls ignited the nearest fuel packages also ignited and the fire travelled backwards before 
flashover occurred. 
The travelling fire was highly influenced by the opening factor. The opening factor of 0.10 m½ allowed the 
travelling fire to proceed for a longer time and at flashover the HRR and temperatures were lower than in 
the scenarios with opening factor 0.05 m½.  
In the scenarios having an opening factor 0.05 m½ the ignition of the walls occurred before impact of the 
travelling fire, suggesting the hot smoke layer to be the triggering factor. In the scenarios with the high 
opening factor 0.10 m½ the walls did not ignite until the travelling fire had approached indicating that the 
larger number of openings released the hot smoke layer and caused surface temperatures below onset 
temperature of 300 °C.   
Increasing the timber amount from 10 % to 20 % accelerated the process leading to a flashover scenario 30 
minutes earlier.  In the case of opening factor 0.05 m½ the increase did not affect the maximum 
0
100
200
300
400
500

700

temperature due to the ventilation-controlled conditions. In the case of opening factor 0.10 m½ the 
temperatures increased as the limit for the ventilation-controlled fire had not been reached.  
A higher opening factor leads to overall lower temperatures in the compartment as more hot gases can be 
released. 
The temperatures show inhomogeneous conditions with maximum temperatures near the travelling fire.  
Local conditions near the combusting walls led to a drop in the oxygen level where combustion of the fuel 
packages could not take place until sufficient air had entrained.  Local regions within a large compartment 
with different conditions than the rest of the compartment should always be assessed.

10 
Conclusion 
The behaviour of travelling fires in a large compartment with height 5 m, floor area 15x45 m² and opening 
factors 0.05 m½ and 0.10 m½ were investigated using CFD-simulations. Multiple scenarios were carried out 
to investigate how the introduction of exposed timber would affect the behaviour of the travelling fire 
under different ventilation conditions.  
All simulations demonstrated that the behaviour of the travelling fire and the overall fire dynamics in the 
compartment was highly influenced by the introduction of exposed timber. How and to what extent 
depends on multiple factors such as the area and location of the timber, the heat release rate of the timber 
and the opening factor. This chapter summarizes the results and conclusions stated in the previous 
sections. For elaboration, please refer to the relevant section.  
General 
• 
All simulations demonstrated that exposed timber in a fire design context causes a more 
unpredictable fire behaviour which should be carefully assessed in any fire design.   
• 
A travelling fire can only occur under the right circumstances regarding compartment geometry, 
openings and fuel load.  
• 
The HRR-curves indicated proportionality between the opening factor and the maximum HRR for 
the ventilation-controlled fire as is the case for small compartments. More data is however 
necessary to make any final conclusions and further research on this topic is highly recommended.  
• 
The gas temperatures in the compartment are inhomogeneous even after flashover.  
• 
Before flashover the maximum temperature follows the location of the travelling fire.  
• 
After flashover the maximum temperatures appear near openings with sufficient oxygen supply.  
• 
The assumption of uniform temperatures in a fire compartment for a fully developed fire is highly 
questioned for a large compartment. Neither the standard fire nor the parametric fire is suitable 
for large, open spaces. 
• 
“One size fits all” do not comply in a large compartment having non-uniform conditions and local 
regions with significantly other premises and ventilation conditions influencing the temperatures 
and oxygen level. 
• 
The effect of increasing the amount of exposed timber strongly depends on the ventilation 
conditions of the compartment.  
• 
Increasing the amount of exposed timber do not influence the HRR and temperatures if the 
conditions for a ventilation-controlled fire is already present. 
• 
Increasing the amount of exposed timber in a compartment where the limit for the ventilation-
controlled fire has not yet been reached will increase the HRR and the maximum temperature.  
• 
Increasing the amount of exposed timber accelerates the fire propagation 
• 
A higher opening factor leads to lower temperatures.  
• 
However, in some scenarios the high opening factor led to more combustion of the fuel leading to a 
higher HRR.  
 
 
==PAGE:122==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
110 
 
 
• 
A maximum of 20 % exposed timber with respect to the total surface area walls and ceiling of the 
compartment do not necessarily provide a safe fire design in a large compartment, where the fire 
dynamics are highly dependent on the location of the timber and the HRR (charring rate). The 20 % 
should be used with caution in large, open spaces as the fire dynamics is significantly altered and 
no models can fully predict the behaviour. Even with an exposed amount of 10 % the fire dynamics 
was altered especially if the timber was located at the ceiling. More data and further investigations 
are however necessary to make any final conclusions.  
Exposed timber at ceiling 
The amount of exposed timber was 10 % and 20 % with respect to the surface area of walls and ceiling in 
the compartment. The heat release rate of the timber was 100 kW/m² and 150 kW/m².  
• 
The behaviour of the travelling fire was significantly changed as the fire turned into a flashover 
scenario shortly after the ignition of the ceiling.  
• 
The worst-case scenario regarding the structures is not the flashover scenario, but the travelling 
fire scenario with a long fire duration where the structures are subject to heat impact for a longer 
period.  
• 
The opening factor had little influence on the ignition time of the ceiling, but the high opening 
factor reduced the fire spread rate at the ceiling. 
• 
Doubling the amount of exposed timber from 10 % to 20 % or increasing the HRR from 100 kW/m² 
to 150 kW/m² accelerated both the fire spread rate at the ceiling and the fire propagation and 
advanced the flashover occurrence with approximately 10 minutes.  
• 
In the scenarios with opening factor 0.10 m½ the fire spread between fuel packages - except in the 
scenario with 20 % exposed timber - ceased. The reason behind this is presumably the altered fire 
dynamics caused by the burning ceiling, but further research is necessary.  
Exposed timber walls 
The amount of exposed timber was 10 % and 20 % with respect to the surface area of walls and ceiling in 
the compartment. The heat release rate of the timber was 100 kW/m². The walls were at the farthest end 
of the compartment from the initial fire.  
• 
All walls ignited almost simultaneously, and flashover occurred within minutes.  
• 
The behaviour of the travelling fire was not affected until ignition of the walls occurred. 
• 
The travelling fire was highly influenced by the opening factor.  
o The opening factor of 0.10 m½ allowed the travelling fire to proceed for a longer time and 
at flashover the HRR and temperatures were lower than in the scenarios with opening 
factor 0.05 m½.  
o In the scenarios having an opening factor 0.05 m½ the heat impact from the hot smoke 
layer was the trigger for ignition.  
o In the scenarios with the high opening factor 0.10 m½ the walls did not ignite until the 
travelling fire had approached.  
 
 
==PAGE:123==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
111

Validation 
In this section the output data from the FDS-simulations are validated. The baseline model B1.1 with no 
exposed timber is used as basis for the validation as there are no available methods to properly validate the 
models with exposed timber.  
11.1

The HRR-curve is illustrated in Figure 11.1. The total energy release Q is

This is the area below the curve. The function f(t) varies and a formula is not possible to assign. Instead, the 
area is calculated by dividing the area into smaller areas corresponding to small time steps, see Figure 11.2. 
The area of each small time step can by calculated by using the trapezoidal rule. For each small timestep

calculated.

11.3 
Gas temperatures 
Based on the results from the FDS-simulations the non-uniform conditions in the large compartment were 
confirmed. There are immediately no simple models available for validation of gas temperatures in large 
compartments with travelling fires and exposed timber as most analytical and empirical based formulas are 
derived for small compartments with uniform conditions. 
11.3.1 Near field temperatures 
For validation of the near field temperature Heskestad formula [42] is applied considering one fuel package. 
The flames cover an area of A=1 m² giving an equal circular diameter of D=1.128 m.

11.3.2 Far field temperatures 
There are no present analytical formulas than can predict the gas temperatures in a large compartment 
with a travelling fire. Recent work “The Improved Travelling Fires Methodology” (iTFM) [73] describes 
analytical equations for the far-field temperatures but in an enclosure with no combustible structures. The 
methodology is based on Alperts formula assuming a ceiling jet. In our scenario B1.1 we do not have at 
ceiling jet and the temperatures from iTFM must be expected to be higher than the FDS-output values.

directly into account and is based on the assumption that the flame impinges the ceiling.

11.5 
Validation - Conclusion 
The output from the baseline scenario B1.1 with opening factor 0.05 m½ and no exposed timber were 
validated against theoretical models. The basic checks of heat release rate and mass loss rate showed very 
fine correspondence with the theoretical values.  
The near field temperature above the plume were validated using Heskestads plume model. The model is 
not directly applicable to this scenario and valid assumptions had to be made. The temperature above the 
plume showed very good correspondence with the Heskestad plume model.  
There are no immediate models to validate the far-field temperatures in this large compartment. The far-
field temperatures were compared to the improved Travelling Fire Methodology iTFM [73] but the values 
did not correspond properly, and the method is not applicable in this case.  
The method using energy balance equations showed very good correspondence between the analytical 
values and the FDS-output for the gas layer temperatures in the stable phase of the fire.  
The FDS-model is now based on the above assumptions considered validated. However, the far-field 
temperature and non-uniform conditions in a large compartment need continuous thorough considerations 
For validation of the temperatures in the far-field other methods than the ones used in this validation 
might be applicable, but to this authors knowledge there is at the moment no appropriate theoretical 
methods available. Instead, full scale experiments must be carried out at a high cost.

12 
Further research topics 
During the making of this thesis interesting areas suitable for further investigation appeared. The author 
highly encourages and recommends these areas being subjected to further research. 
• 
The simulations revealed unexpectedly a fire cease of the fuel packages when exposed timber at 
the ceiling were introduced. A final conclusion regarding this cease of fire was not possible to 
make. Further investigations regarding the complex fire dynamics of a burning combustible ceiling 
in a large compartment is recommended.  
• 
Due to the limited time frame of the thesis the number of investigated scenarios had to be limited. 
Even though they are representative any alteration of the parameters will give other results. As the 
opening factor and amount and location of the exposed timber highly affects the fire behaviour 
other scenarios are highly recommended to be subjected to investigation.  
• 
The scenarios indicated proportionality between the opening factor and the maximum HRR of the 
ventilation-controlled fire. More data is however necessary to make any final conclusions.  
• 
The large compartment showed non-uniform conditions and local regions with other premises than 
in the rest of the compartment. Is should be investigated whether it is possible to design models for 
large compartments taking local conditions with individual fire dynamics into account.  
• 
In the Danish pre-accepted solutions an exposed surface area of the timber structures of 20 % with 
respect to the total surface area of the walls and ceiling in the compartment is allowed. This is 
based on data for small compartments. It should be investigated if this amount is still a safe design 
for large, open spaces.  
• 
The classification by furnace testing of a load-bearing combustible structure is based on the same 
premises as a noncombustible structure. This is highly debatable since the behaviour of the 
element and exposure during the test are different. Despite the topic is already being investigated 
the author encourages further investigation.

---FUEL LOAD--- 
Fuel packages (wood cribs) each with dimensions 1.0 m x 1.0 m x 1.0 m 
Evenly distributed on the floor with spacing 1.0 m 
 
X-DIRECTION: 22 CRIBS 
Y-DIRECTION: 7 CRIBS 
TOTAL: 154 CRIBS 
 
Each fuel package is named FPa.b, where 'a' is the number of the row in the x-
direction and 'b' is the number of the row in the y-direction. 
 
---INITIAL FIRE--- 
Fire starts at FP1.4 at t = 0 s

14.3 
Appendix C: Preliminary studies 
Maximum heat release rate 
The maximum heat release rate for the fuel-controlled fire depends on the fuel properties and the 
geometry and ventilation conditions. Values for the maximum heat release for fire design purposes for 
different types of occupancies can be found in the national codes and in the literature.  
According to the Danish guidelines for fire dimensioning [66], the minimum design fire load for shops is  
𝑄̇ = 10 MW and for offices 𝑄̇ = 5 𝑀𝑊. These values should always be carefully assessed and adjusted 
according to the actual conditions in the building. They cannot without further investigation be applied to a 
large compartment.   
When determining the heat release rate for the FDS-model some issues should be considered. First, the 
HRR must represent a realistic fire load in the building. Secondly, in a large compartment with large 
openings, the HRR must be large enough to secure a fire spread to all fuel packages. A small HRR will not be 
able to ignite the fuel packages located close to the openings as they get cooled by air entrainment. On the 
other hand, if the HRR is very large, the fire quickly gets under-ventilated, or the hot smoke layer quickly 
builds up and ignites all the fuel packages in the compartment simultaneously which hinders a travelling 
fire scenario. This is however strongly dependent on the actual geometry and ventilation conditions of the 
compartment.  
In the literature a wide range of design values for heat release rate pr. unit area (HRRPUA) can be found. 
Some of these are based on fires in real scenarios [75]. Based on literature review by Hopkin et al. [76] the 
range for shops is HRRPUA = 270 – 1,200 kW/m². This is quite a wide range due to the fact, that ‘shops’ can 
have all kind of goods.  For offices the range is HRRPUA = 150 – 650 kW/m². In the Eurocode the HRR for 
offices is HRR = 250 kW/m². This indicates that a standard value does not exist as many factors influence 
the HRR, and a conservative value should always be considered in the fire design strategy.  
Fire load density 
The fire load density in a compartment will depend on the type of occupancy and whether the 
compartment is used as either an industrial building, a storage facility, a supermarket, a shopping center or 
an open space office.  
In the national codes fire load densities for common occupancies can be found. According to Danish 
National Annex to Eurocode 1991-1-2  [51] the design fire load for offices could be taken as  
qt,d = 200 MJ/m2 (related to the total surface area At) giving a total amount of energy of Q = 390 GJ and 
according to  DS/EN 1991-1-2 [50] qd,k = 420 MJ/m2 (related to the floor area). 
For a shopping center the characteristic fire load is qf,k = 600 MJ/m² (with respect to the floor area Af) 
according to DS/EN 1991-1-2 [50] giving a total amount of energy of Q = 405 GJ. This characteristic value 
must be multiplied with factors according to the fire activation risk and the combustion factor to find the 
design value of the fire load density.  
For a wide variety of occupancies, the corresponding fire load densities can be found in the International 
Fire Engineering Guidelines [67].  
==PAGE:212==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
200 
 
 
In  Table 14.2 the values are summarized. These values should however be used with caution and their 
scalability to large compartments should be assessed. In a small compartment a higher fire load density 
(MJ/m²) could be expected than in a large compartment giving the larger floor area in the latter with the 
opportunity for more open spaces. Also it should be noted that some of the values might rely on older data 
for furnishings. In modern contemporary buildings these values might no longer apply. This is however not 
investigated further in this thesis but should be assessed in a real fire design.

Observations based on the preliminary studies 
Based on the above listed values for HRR and total fire loads, several simulations were carried out to “tune” 
to model to find the optimal conditions for a travelling fire. It is important to emphasize, that in a real fire 
scenario, these conditions may not prevail, but the scope of this thesis is to investigate the behaviour of 
travelling fires.  
The tuning of the model was done without any exposed timber present.  
In the following the HRRPUA is related to the surface of the burner (fuel package).  
The simulations revealed that the fire behaviour in the large compartment is highly dependent on the 
ventilation conditions, the heat release rate and total amount of fuel. The sensitivity analysis carried out 
revealed that the mesh size (cell size) also is a very important factor.  
The observations showed that for a low opening factor (O < 0.08 m½) and a HRRPUA > 400 kW/m² the heat 
release and building of a hot smoke layer was very dominant resulting in high temperatures and ignition of 
multiple fuel packages simultaneously. This led to a very high increase in the HRR, a flashover scenario and 
a ventilation-controlled fire. The same phenomenon was observed for a low opening factor where cold air 
entrainment was limited resulting in high temperatures in the compartment.  
On the other hand, a high opening factor (O> 0.08 m½) and a low HRRPUA of 250 kW/m² were not able to 
sustain a high enough temperature in the compartment to ensure fire spread between the fuel packages, 
and the fire did not proceed through the compartment.  
In Figure 14.1 different HRR-curves for the preliminary studies and the final choice of the travelling fire 
scenario are illustrated.  
==PAGE:213==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
201 
 
 
In Figure 14.2 and Figure 14.3 Smokeview outputs visualize the difference in the fire behaviour for 
HRRPUA=500 kW/m2 and the travelling fire scenario. For the travelling fire scenario, the total amount of 
fuel was taken as half the tabulated value for a shopping center of 405 GJ.  
 
 
Figure 14.1 HRR-curves for different occupancies, HRRPUA and total fire loads Q. Opening factor 
O=0.10 m½.  For HRRPUA > 400 kW/m2 the high temperatures and hot smoke layer leads to ignition 
of multiple fuel packages at the same time giving a very high HRR. With HRRPUA = 250 kW/m2 the 
fire is not strong enough to sustain burning and the fire ceases. The HRR-curve for a travelling fire 
scenario with HRRPUA=300 kW/m2, Q=203 GJ and opening factor O=0.10 m½ shows stable 
conditions as the fire travels through the compartment.  
 
Figure 14.2 HRRPUA= 500 kW/m². All fuel packages are involved in the fire. Flames outside openings 
indicate a flashover scenario and a ventilation-controlled fire.

Figure 14.3 HRRPUA 300 kW/m². Only a few fuel packages are involved in the fire (red 
highlighted cubes). Brown cubes indicate fuel packages not yet involved in the fire. The fire 
moves through the compartment leaving burnt fuel behind (black squares) and igniting new 
fuel. This is the characteristics of a travelling fire. Steady state conditions apply. Initial fire 
illustrated as a red cube in the middle of the first row (FP1.4).  
The maximum heat release rate for the ventilation-controlled fire, see section 6.3, is 
𝐻𝑅𝑅𝑉𝐶=  0.09 ∗𝐴𝑜√𝐻𝑜∗∆𝐻𝑐,𝑤𝑜𝑜𝑑 
This formula was derived by Kawagoe based on experiments for very small enclosures [77]. It cannot be 
used for large compartments without further investigation.  
If we try to use it for this large compartment with O=0.10 m½ we find 
𝐻𝑅𝑅𝑉𝐶=  0.09 ∗84 𝑚2 ∗√4 ∗17,500 𝑘𝐽/𝑘𝑔 ≅ 300 MW 
This is much larger than the maximum HRR we found in Figure 14.1 for shopping centers, which should 
indicate that a fuel-controlled fire could take place. This was not the case, and the formula does not apply 
for large compartments, as expected.  
When exposed timber is present the fire load will increase significantly. This will lead to a scenario where 
the fire gets ventilation-controlled if the openings are limited. 
The preliminary studies and simulations are not discussed further in this thesis as their only purpose was to 
tune the model within reasonable limits.

14.4 
Appendix D: Investigation of fire spread cease in Scenario C2.2 
In scenario C2.2 in section 9.16.3.3 the fire spread was not supported even with a fine mesh resolution of 
δx = 0.100 m. In this appendix this behaviour is further investigated.  
Cease of fire spread between the fuel packages 
Increasing the fire load by having exposed timber present should intuitively give a more vigorous fire. Since 
the mesh resolution in this case was not the determining factor, further investigation is needed.  
 
 
 
Figure 14.4 Figure 14.5 Scenario C2.2. The fire spread between fuel packages has ceased and only the ceiling is 
burning at t = 67 min.  
 
Oxygen level 
Investigating the oxygen level slices from the Smokeview visualization shows also in this case that there is a 
sufficient level to support burning so this is ruled out as the reason for the fire cease, see Figure 14.6. 
 
Figure 14.6 Oxygen level at t = 36 minutes when the fuel package fire spread starts to cease. There is sufficient oxygen at 
the fuel package level at the bottom to support ignition.  
 
 
FP4.1 
FP5.1 
Door 1.1 
==PAGE:216==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
204 
 
 
Surface temperature of fuel packages 
To investigate the temperature conditions near the cease of the fire spread further, the surface 
temperatures of the fuel packages FP4.1 (face x1) and FP5.1 (face y1) were analyzed, see Figure 14.4. Face 
x1 is facing the burning fuel package in row 3 and face y1 is facing the nearest opening at y=0 m. The 
surface temperatures were compared with the baseline scenario B2.2, see Figure 14.7. 
 
 
Figure 14.7 Surface temperatures of fuel packages in scenario C2.2 with 10 % exposed timber compared to the 
baseline scenario with no timber.  
Figure 14.7 shows that the baseline scenario B2.2 yields higher surface temperatures even before the fuel 
packages ignite (burner start), and the introduction of timber leads to reduced surface temperatures. This 
is not immediately explainable and further research is needed.  
Mass flow through openings 
We know that the burning timber at the ceiling causes more hot gases leaving the compartment and thus 
more cold air to entrain (mass balance). The fire spread stops just at the first opening substantiating this 
assumption.  
To investigate this further, the FDS-input file was supplied with command lines to extract data for the mass 
flow through the openings, see section 9.9.4. The opening is split in 4 areas each with a height of 1 m and a 
width of 3 m to measure at more points along the z-axis. The positive values are for the positive y-direction 
and indicates mass entering the compartment. The opening is door 1.1, see Figure 14.5 
Note: To save computational time the models B1.2 and C1.2 with cell size 0.125 m were used for this 
purpose. This is from a qualitative point of view acceptable. 
As indicated in Figure 14.8 there is no significant deviation in the mass flow from the baseline scenario. It 
was expected to see an increase for the mass flow for the scenario C1.2, but this was not the case. The 
mass flow did not reveal any reason for the fire spread to cease.

Figure 14.8 Comparison of mass flow through opening (Door 1.1) in facade at y=0 m for baseline scenario B1.2 and 
scenario C1.2. Mass flow measured through opening with height 4 m and width 3 m. Positive values indicate mass 
entering the compartment and negative values indicate mass leaving the compartment.  
Smoke layer 
The burning timber ceiling produces large amounts of smoke. In full scale experiments with exposed CLT-
ceilings, this dense smoke layer inhibited the combustion of the ceiling and reduced the impact of the 
ground fuel [78]. It is unclear if FDS can simulate this behaviour and thus if this was a contributing factor to 
the fire spread cease.  
It was unfortunately not possible to give a plausible explanation of the fire spread cease in scenario C2.2 
and further research is highly recommended.
